منهجي في الإعداد والتحقيق (1) شمل عملي شرح السلم المرونق في علم المنطق للأخضري والشرح قد نشر من قبل عدة مرات تارة بصفة مستقلة، وتارة أخرى مصحوب بحواشي، وقد اخترت من الطبعات القديمة طبعة بولاق وبهامشها شرح الدمنهوري المسمى (إيضاح المبهم من معاني السلم) ورأيت فيها ما يساعدني على إخراج النص سليم ودقيق فلهذا فقد دققت المقابلة بين النسخ المطبوعة والمخطوطتين واستعنت على جملة من المعاجم اللغوية والمنطقية كالتوقيف على مهمات التعاريف للمناوي والتعريفات للشريف الجرجاني اللذان استفدت منهما كثيراً في إخراج النص ومعرفة المصطلحات المنطقية، كما لا يفوتني ذكر شرح العلامة عبدالرحيم فرج الجندي المفتش بالمعاهد الأزهرية سابقاً والمدرس بالدراسات الإسلامية بالقسم العالي بالأزهر حالياً. وذلك في كتابه القيم «شرح السلم». وكذا مغني الطلاب شرح متن إيساغوجي»، للعلامة أثير الدين الأبهري تحقيق محمود رمضان البوطي، وقد قمت بالتعليق على جميع المنطقية التي تناولها الشيخ الأخضري في شرح سلمه، كما خرجت المصطلحات (1) قلت وقد توسع الشيخ بشير ضيف بن أبي بكر عن السلم في كتابه (عقود الجواهر في الكشف عن مخطوطات علماء الجزائر) – كتب المنطق - ج 1 - مخطوط . وكذلك في كتابه العقود اللؤلؤية في الكشف عن المطبوعات العربية الجزائرية) قسم المنطق ج 2 وانظر فهرست معلمة التراث الجزائري بين القديم والحديث 198 للشيخ بشير ضيف مراجعة وتقديم عثمان بدري - الجزائر ج الثالث ص 23 أمكنني من الأبيات الشعرية والتراجم والأحاديث والآيات القرآنية ووضعت معجماً للمصطلحات المنطقية مرتب على حروف المعجم. 24
My methodology in preparation and verification: (1) My work included the commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawniq in Logic by al-Akhdari, and the commentary has been published several times, sometimes independently and other times accompanied by glosses. I chose from the old editions the Bulaq print, with the commentary of al-Damanhuri titled 'Idah al-Mubham min Ma'ani al-Sullam' in its margin, and I saw in it what helps me to produce the text sound and accurate. For this I carefully compared the printed copies and the two manuscripts, and I sought help from a number of linguistic and logical dictionaries, such as al-Tawqif 'ala Muhimmat al-Ta'arif by al-Manawi and al-Ta'rifat by al-Sharif al-Jurjani, from which I greatly benefited in producing the text and knowing the logical terminology. I also mention the commentary of the scholar Abd al-Rahim Faraj al-Jundi, former inspector of al-Azhar institutes and currently teacher of Islamic studies in the higher section of al-Azhar, in his valuable book 'Sharh al-Sullam', as well as 'Mughni al-Tullab Sharh Matn Isaghuji' by the scholar Athir al-Din al-Abhari, edited by Mahmud Ramadan al-Buti. I have commented on all the logical topics that Shaykh al-Akhdari addressed in his commentary on al-Sullam, and I extracted the terminology. (1) I say: Shaykh Bashir Dayf ibn Abi Bakr has expanded on al-Sullam in his book 'Uqud al-Jawahir fi Kashf Makhtutat Ulama al-Jaza'ir' – Logic books – vol. 1 – manuscript, and likewise in his book 'al-Uqud al-Lu'lu'iyya fi Kashf al-Matbu'at al-Arabiyya al-Jaza'iriyya', section on Logic, vol. 2. And see Fihrist Ma'lamat al-Turath al-Jaza'iri bayn al-Qadim wa al-Hadith, p. 198, by Shaykh Bashir Dayf, reviewed and presented by Uthman Badri, Algeria, part 3, p. 23. I was able to [gather] poetic verses, biographies, hadiths, and Quranic verses, and I compiled a glossary of logical terms arranged alphabetically. 24.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction, explaining his methodology in preparing and verifying the text of al-Akhdari's commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawniq. He states that his work includes both the original commentary and his own additions. He selected the Bulaq print as his base text because it includes al-Damanhuri's gloss, which aids in producing a sound and accurate edition. He then carefully compared this print with two manuscripts and consulted linguistic and logical dictionaries, specifically al-Tawqif by al-Manawi and al-Ta'rifat by al-Jurjani, to understand the terminology. He also acknowledges the works of later scholars: Abd al-Rahim Faraj al-Jundi's 'Sharh al-Sullam' and Athir al-Din al-Abhari's 'Mughni al-Tullab', edited by Mahmud Ramadan al-Buti. The editor notes that he has commented on all logical topics covered by al-Akhdari and extracted the terminology. In a footnote, he mentions Bashir Dayf's works on Algerian manuscripts and printed books, providing a reference. Finally, he states that he collected poetic verses, biographies, hadiths, and Quranic verses, and compiled an alphabetical glossary of logical terms.

Author states his use of poetry, hadith, and Quranic verses and a dictionary.

Learning Objective

The author anchors logic in Islamic tradition using scriptural and literary sources plus a dictionary.

In this brief front matter passage, the author tells us about his sources and a practical aid he provides for students. He draws on poetic verses, biographical works, hadith, and Quranic verses, showing that logic is not isolated from the Islamic sciences. The Nine Preliminaries of Logic
Author lists nine headings for introducing logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall and list the nine preliminary topics that the author uses to introduce the science of logic, and explain the purpose of each in the traditional framework.

Core Idea
Logic's introduction is built on nine compressed headings—master them first.

The author begins his work by laying out the nine standard headings under which he will present the science of logic. These nine terms are the skeleton of the introductory section. Committing them to memory now gives you a mental map for the detailed definitions and explanations that follow in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read and pronounce the nine terms in Arabic
Slowly read the list from the Arabic text: تعريف، موضوع، فائدة، ثمرة، نسبة، واضع، اسم، استمداد، مسائل.
These are the standard headings in many Islamic sciences.
2
Group the terms by function
Notice that some headings relate to the essence of the science (تعريف, موضوع, اسم, مسائل), others to its value (فائدة, ثمرة), and others to its historical context (نسبة, واضع, استمداد).
This grouping aids memorization.
3
Match each Arabic term to its English translation
Ensure you can pair each Arabic word with its precise English equivalent: تعریف = definition, موضوع = subject matter, فائدة = benefit, etc.
Use the translation provided.
4
Create a mnemonic device
Invent an acronym or phrase from the first letters or key words to recall the order. For example, 'D (definition) S (subject) B (benefit) F (fruit) R (relation) F (founder) N (name) D (derivation) I (issues)'.
Share your mnemonic with a study partner.
5
Test yourself by writing the list from memory
Write the nine terms in Arabic without looking. Then check against the original. Repeat until error-free.
This is a memorization anchor: drill it until automatic.

This passage enumerates the nine headings under which the author will present the science of logic. He begins with the definition of the science, then lists its subject matter, its benefit, its fruit, its relation, its founder, its name, its derivation, and finally its issues. The compression and sequential ordering indicate that this is a matn-style skeleton: the student is expected to commit these nine topics to memory first, knowing that each heading will be unpacked and defined in the detailed lessons that follow. The passage thus serves as a roadmap for the entire introductory section.

The Definition and Subject Matter of Logic
Definition and subject matter of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of logic by identifying its genus and differentia, and distinguish its subject matter from its definition.

Core Idea
Logic is the instrument that elevates known concepts and judgments to what was unknown.

Every science begins with a precise definition that sets its boundaries and focus. Here, the author gives the definition of logic in terms of its object of investigation—conceptual and assentive knowables—and its purpose: leading to the unknown. We will dismantle this definition to see its logical structure and identify its subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of logic
The definition begins with 'علم' (science). This is the broader category logic belongs to. Any science is a body of systematic knowledge.
In Arabic, 'علم' can mean either knowledge or science; here it means a discipline.
2
Extract the differentia
The differentia is the phrase that distinguishes logic from other sciences: 'يبحث فيه عن المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية من حيث أنها توصل إلى معرفة مجهول'. That is, it investigates conceptual and assentive knowables insofar as they lead to knowledge of the unknown.
The key is 'من حيث أنها توصل' (insofar as they lead). Logic studies knowables from a functional perspective, not their essence.
3
Distinguish the subject matter from the definition
The subject matter is explicitly stated as 'المعلوم التصوري والمعلوم التصديقي من هذه الحيثية'. This is the same as the differentia but restated as the object of study.
The definition includes the genus and differentia; the subject matter is the specific aspect of the objects studied.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider the conceptual knowledge of 'wudu' (ablution) as a definition, and the assentive knowledge that 'wudu is obligatory' as a judgment. Logic studies how from known definitions and known rulings we can infer new rulings, e.g., 'wudu is required for salah'.

Source Text

Definition of the science of logic: It is a science in which one investigates the conceptual and assentive knowables insofar as they lead to knowledge of an unknown conceptual — so it is called a clarifying, defining statement — or lead to an unknown assentive — so it is called a proof. And its subject matter: the conceptual knowable and the assentive knowable from this aspect. And its benefit: safeguarding the mind from error in thought. And its fruit: the ability to establish proofs and demonstrations and to defend true beliefs, so the servant attains eternal felicity. And its relation to other sciences: its clarification of them, and it is a means of verifying them and defending their principles. And its originator: the ancient philosophers such as Aristotle and others. And its source: the intellect. And its issues: its propositions related to definitions and syllogisms.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the introductory matn of the science of logic, enumerating the standard preliminaries that every student must anchor in memory before advancing to the details of definition and syllogism. The author begins with the formal definition: logic is the science that investigates conceptual knowables and assentive knowables insofar as they lead to knowledge of an unknown. When the unknown is conceptual, the expression that conveys this knowledge is called a clarifying, defining statement; when the unknown is assentive, it is called a proof. He then identifies the subject matter: the conceptual and assentive knowables precisely under this functional aspect of leading to the unknown. Next he states the benefit: the safeguarding of the mind from error in thought. He follows with the fruit: the capacity to construct proofs and demonstrations and to defend true beliefs, by which the servant attains eternal felicity. He then clarifies the relation of logic to other sciences: logic elucidates them, acts as a means to verify them, and defends their foundational principles. He attributes the founding of the science to the ancient philosophers such as Aristotle. He identifies its source as the intellect. Finally, he outlines its issues: the propositions pertaining to definitions and syllogisms.

The Benefit and Fruit of Logic
Benefit and fruit of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the benefit (safeguarding the mind) and the fruit (capacity to construct proofs and attain felicity) of logic, and apply logic's benefit to a theological argument.

Core Idea
Logic protects the mind from error during reasoning and yields the ability to defend truth, leading to eternal happiness.

The author lists two closely related but distinct rewards of studying logic: its immediate benefit and its ultimate fruit. Understanding this distinction helps students appreciate why logic is not just an abstract exercise but a tool for sound thinking and religious defense. We will break down each concept and see how they connect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the benefit (fāʾida) of logic
The benefit is 'عصمة الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر', protecting the mind from error in thought. This is the direct, immediate advantage: using logic prevents mistakes in reasoning.
Note that 'ʿiṣma' means preservation or infallibility, but here it means a safeguard, not absolute infallibility.
2
Identify the fruit (thamara) of logic
The fruit is 'القدرة على إقامة الحجج والبراهين والدفاع عن العقائد الحقة فيفوز العبد بالسعادة الأبدية'. This is the ultimate outcome: the ability to construct proofs and defend true beliefs, resulting in eternal felicity.
The fruit builds on the benefit: a mind safeguarded from error is capable of constructing sound proofs.
3
Contrast benefit vs. fruit
The benefit is a negative protection (avoiding error), while the fruit is a positive product (constructing proofs). Both are essential but distinct.
In Islamic sciences, many disciplines have a fāʾida (immediate use) and a thamara (ultimate goal). For example, grammar's benefit is correct speech; its fruit is understanding the Qur'an.
4
Apply to a kalam argument
Consider the kalām proof for the existence of God: using a valid syllogism from the temporality of the world to a creator. Logic's benefit ensures the mind avoids fallacies in that argument; its fruit enables the mutakallim to convincingly establish the proof and defend it, leading to reward.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Logic's Relation to Other Sciences, Origin, Source, and Scope
Relation, origin, source, and issues of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain logic's instrumental role among the Islamic sciences, identify its origin as the intellect, and list its two main issues: definitions and syllogisms.

Core Idea
Logic is the servant of all Islamic sciences—it clarifies, verifies, and defends their principles without having subject matter of its own.

Having established what logic is and why it is beneficial, the author locates logic in the broader landscape of knowledge. He defines its relation to other sciences, its founder, its source, and its main topics. This lesson will help you see how logic fits into your study of fiqh, kalam, tafsir, and other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify logic's relation to other sciences
The text says logic is 'مبيانة لها' (clarifier/elucidator for them) and 'وسيلة لتحقيقها والدفاع عن قواعدها' (a means to verify them and defend their principles). Logic does not have its own subject matter independent of other sciences; it serves them.
This is a key difference from most sciences, which have their own unique subjects (e.g., fiqh studies acts of worship, tafsir studies the Qur'an).
2
Identify the originator (wāḍiʿ)
The author attributes the founding of logic to the ancient philosophers like Aristotle ('الفلاسفة الأقدمون كأرسطو وغيره'). This is a historical attribution.
Later scholars like Ibn Taymiyya challenged this, arguing that sound reasoning is innate, but the matn records the standard view.
3
Identify the source (istimdād) of logic
The source is 'العقل' (the intellect). Logic is derived from rational principles, not from revelation or empirical observation.
This means logic's rules are universal and not culturally bound.
4
Identify the issues (masāʾil) of logic
The two main issues are 'قضاياه المتعلقة بالتعريفات وبالأقيسة' (its propositions related to definitions and syllogisms). These correspond to the two types of knowables: conceptual (definitions) and assentive (syllogisms).
The entire rest of the science is a detailed study of these two topics.

Introduction and Front Matter

Founder, Source, and Subject Matter of Logic
Author enumerates the founder, source, and subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the founder (wadi‘), source (istimdad), and subject matter (masa’il) of logic as presented in the Sullam al-Mantiq.

Core Idea
Logic is an intellectual science, not a transmitted one; its foundation is the mind, not revelation.

Before diving into definitions and syllogisms, the Sullam al-Mantiq introduces the science of logic under three standard headings: its founder, its source of derivation, and its issues. These headings help us locate logic within the map of the Islamic sciences. Unlike Fiqh or Hadith, logic is not derived from revelation but from pure reason. Understanding this will prevent confusion between transmitted and intellectual disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the founder (wadi‘)
Find the term 'wadi‘' in the Arabic. The author attributes the founding of logic to the 'ancient philosophers' (al-falasifah al-aqdamun), naming Aristotle as an example. In Islamic sciences, the founder is the one who codified the discipline; here it is the Greek philosophical tradition.
Contrast with Usul al-Fiqh, where al-Shafi‘i is the founder.
2
Identify the source (istimdad)
Locate 'istimdad' in the passage. The author states logic is derived 'from the intellect' (min al-‘aql). This means that the principles and evidence of logic are accessed through reason alone, not through textual transmission (naql).
This distinguishes logic from sciences like Tafsir or Fiqh, which rely on Qur’an and Sunnah.
3
Identify the subject matter (masa’il)
Find 'masa’il' and the phrase 'qadaya… al-muta‘alliqah bi al-ta‘rifat wa bi al-aqisah'. The subject matter of logic consists of propositions (qadaya) related to two main topics: definitions (ta‘rifat) and syllogisms (aqisah). This tells you that logic studies how to form correct definitions and how to construct valid arguments.
The plural aqisah (syllogisms) is a variant of aqyas; both refer to the same thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وواضعه الفلاسفة الأقدمون كأرسطو وغيره. واستمداده من العقل. ومسائله قضاياه المتعلقة بالتعريفات وبالأقيسة.
And its founder: the ancient philosophers such as Aristotle and others. And its derivation: from the intellect. And its issues: its propositions related to definitions and to syllogisms.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage introduces the science of logic by enumerating three standard descriptive headings used in the classification of sciences: its founder (wadi'), its source of derivation (istimdad), and its issues or subject matter (masa'il). The author states that the founders of logic were the ancient philosophers such as Aristotle, indicating the historical origins of this discipline in the Greek tradition before its integration into Islamic learning. He then notes that logic derives its evidence from the intellect (al-'aql), distinguishing it from transmitted sciences such as Fiqh or Hadith which derive from naql. Finally, he defines the issues of logic as its propositions (qadaya) pertaining to definitions (ta'rifat) and syllogisms (aqisah), thereby circumscribing the field: logic teaches how to construct correct definitions and how to form valid arguments from them. The student should note that while Usul al-Fiqh has al-Shafi'i as its codifier and the Qur'an and Sunnah as its sources, Mantiq has the ancient philosophers as its codifiers and the intellect as its sole source.

Key Terms
واضع
استمداد
مسائل
قضايا
تعريفات
أقيسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Qur'an and the Sunnah
B) The intellect (al-'aql)
C) Empirical observation of nature
D) The consensus of the jurists

Key Takeaway
Logic is an intellectual science founded by ancient philosophers, derived from reason, and concerned with definitions and syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the founder, source, and issues of Ilm al-Kalam (theology based on reason and scripture) using the same three headings. How does it differ from logic?
?
Compare the source of logic (intellect) with the source of Fiqh (Qur’an and Sunnah). In what types of discussions would each source be authoritative?

The Purpose of a Commentary: Author's Introduction
Author introduces his commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main components of a classical Islamic scholarly introduction: basmala, hamdala, and the statement of purpose.

Core Idea
A preface explains not only the content but the author's motivation and pedagogical intent.

In this lesson, we examine the opening lines of the commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author begins with the traditional formula, then explains why he wrote the commentary. This preface is not just decoration; it clarifies the audience, purpose, and pedagogical approach. Understanding the preface helps you, as a student, grasp what the author expects you to learn and how he intends to teach it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional opening phrases
Look for the basmala ('In the name of Allah'), hamdala ('Praise be to Allah'), and salawat ('blessings and peace upon the Prophet'). These are standard in Islamic scholarly works and establish the religious frame.
2
Find the statement of purpose
After the opening, the author says 'When I composed the poem... students requested from me ... to place for them upon it a beneficial commentary.' This tells you why the commentary was written.
3
Note the author's humility and reliance on Allah
The author says 'if I were capable, I would have done more, but I rely on Allah.' This shows the traditional scholarly attitude of humility and dependence on divine aid.
4
Connect to the pedagogical aim
The commentary is meant to 'safeguard what it contains of meanings and doubts that arise.' The author is addressing potential confusion, so the commentary is a tool for clarity.

The author acknowledges his reliance on Allah for success and humbly notes his limitations. This introduction sets the tone for a pedagogical work aimed at clarifying logical concepts for students.

Reading the Author's Introduction (Muqaddima)
Author introduces himself and states his purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural components of a classical Islamic scholarly introduction and explain the author's stated purpose.

Core Idea
Every classical text begins with a muqaddima that reveals authorship, purpose, and method.

Before diving into logical definitions and syllogisms, it is essential to understand the frame in which the author presents his work. This passage from al-Akhdari's commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq follows the standard pattern of a scholarly muqaddima: praise of God, testimony of tawhid, salawat on the Prophet, and a clear statement of purpose. Mastering this frame helps you situate any classical text in its scholarly context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author
Locate the author's full name and honorifics at the beginning of the passage. Note the chain of titles (Shaykh, Imam, Allamah) that indicate his scholarly standing.
The name is given as Abu Zayd Abd al-Rahman al-Akhdari.
2
Analyse the Doxology (Basmala, Hamdala, Shahada)
Observe the opening formula: praise to God and testimony of His oneness. This is a standard invocation, but it also asserts the tawhidic foundation of the work.
3
Locate the Prophetic Salutation
Find the salawat upon the Prophet and his Companions. This shows the author's adherence to the sunna and traditional methodology.
The text includes 'sayyidina wa mawlana'.
4
Extract the Stated Purpose
The key phrase is 'wa ba'd' (after which). Read the statement of purpose: the author wrote a commentary to unfold the meanings of his didactic poem for students.
Purpose: 'to place a beneficial commentary upon it'.
5
Note the Work's Title and Form
The original composition is called 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' and is described as an 'urjuza' (didactic poem). Recognising the poetic form is crucial for understanding the commentary's style.
Urjuza indicates a mnemonic teaching tool.

He then sends blessings upon the Prophet, referring to him as our master, lord, and beloved, and includes the Prophet's companions in that salutation. Parsing the Preface: Structure and Epistemic Framing
Author opens with hamdala and turns to the book's subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the structural components of an Islamic logical preface and extract its key rhetorical and epistemic claims despite textual corruption.

Core Idea
A preface is not decoration—it frames the epistemic authority and purpose of the work.

The preface (khutba) of any classical Islamic work is a carefully crafted gateway. It establishes the author's credentials, signals the work's purpose, and often embeds key metaphors that hint at the author's methodology. In this lesson, we will parse the surviving fragments of al-Akhdar's preface to al-Sullam, learning how to identify the standard components and extract meaning even from a corrupt text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard components
Locate the basmala (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم), the hamdala (الحمد لله), the shahada (أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله), the salawat (اللهم صل على...), and the transition to the subject. These are the expected building blocks of a classical preface.
Even if some phrases are corrupted, the sequence and keywords can be identified.
2
Analyze the hamdala metaphor
The hamdala contains a metaphor: hearts of scholars are skies, knowledge is suns shining in them. This is not mere praise—it elevates the epistemic status of scholars and frames knowledge as luminous, divine, and expansive.
Compare this metaphor to similar ones in other prefaces (e.g., al-Ghazali's Ihya).
3
Note the function of each component
Each component has a function: basmala invokes divine aid, hamdala praises God for the gift of knowledge, shahada affirms tawhid, salawat honors the Prophet, and the transition signals the book's subject. Together they establish the work's Islamic framework.
4
Handle textual corruption
When OCR corruption obscures parts, focus on clear keywords and the overall arc. The metaphor and the mention of 'al-Sullam' are clear enough to reconstruct the preface's intent.
Use context and standard formulas to infer missing content.

The Scholar's Heart as a Heaven: Understanding the Preface of al-Sullam
Author opens with a prefatory sermon elevating the science before technical discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural components of a classical Islamic scholarly preface and explain the metaphor of 'hearts of scholars as heavens' in the context of Ilm al-Mantiq.

Core Idea
A preface elevates the science before it is taught.

Before diving into the technical definitions and syllogisms of logic, the author opens with a khutbah that sets the spiritual and intellectual frame. This preface is not merely decorative; it establishes the reverence for knowledge and the station of scholars. Understanding this frame helps you read the rest of the text with the proper mindset.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the classical preface structure
Identify the basmala (بسم الله), hamdala (الحمد لله), shahada (أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله), and salawat (صلى الله عليه). These are standard opening elements that affirm tawhid and prophetic reverence.
This structure is used across Islamic texts, from fiqh to logic.
2
Analyze the key metaphor
The author says God made the hearts of scholars heavens in which the suns of knowledge shine. What does this imply about the scholar's role and the nature of knowledge? Consider how this metaphor dignifies the science of logic by connecting it to divine illumination.
Metaphors like this are common in prefaces to stress the sacredness of learning.
3
Identify the transition to the work
Look for the phrase where the author turns from praise to announcing his book: 'فلما وضعت ... إلى المسائل ... السلم'. This signals the start of the didactic content.
The corruption makes this part incomplete, but the intent is clear.
4
Note the incomplete portions
Observe the [؟] markers in the Arabic: these indicate corrupted or missing text. Recognize that your understanding of the preface must be tentative until a verified copy is consulted.
This teaches you to handle manuscript variants critically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم [؟] قال الشيخ العالم [؟] الحمد لله الذي جعل قلوب العلماء سماوات تتجلى فيها شموس المعارف [؟] وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له الرب الكريم [؟] الذي تقدس وتعالى عن أن يحاط [؟] مجده وعظيم جلاله [؟] صلى الله عليه وعلى آله [؟] فلما وضعت [؟] إلى المسائل [؟] السلم [؟] علم [؟] والحمد لله [؟] وأنعم الله على سيدنا محمد السراج [؟] [؟] وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم [؟]

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface (khutbah) to the work. He opens with the basmala and praise of God, employing the well-known metaphor that the hearts of scholars are heavens in which the suns of knowledge shine. He then testifies to divine oneness and transcendence, asserting that the Lord is exalted above being fully encompassed by human praise of His glory and majesty. After invoking blessings upon the Prophet and his family, he turns to the work itself, alluding to the laying of foundations for the questions of the ladder (al-sullam) leading to a science. The passage closes with further blessings upon the Prophet, described with an honorific, and upon his family and companions. The severe corruption in the latter half prevents full reconstruction, yet the overall architecture remains visible: it follows the classical pattern of praising God, invoking the Prophet, and announcing the subject.

Identifying the Treatise and Its Author
Editor introduces the authenticated text with its title and author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the full title and author of the foundational logic treatise al-Sullam al-Murawnaq and explain the significance of its name in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
The Ornamented Ladder is the gateway to Islamic logic.

This lesson introduces the first page of the authentic edition of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. Although the manuscript colophon is damaged, the editor's heading clearly records the treatise's full title and its author. Learning to correctly identify a primary text is a fundamental skill in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Locate the editor's heading
In the passage, the editor writes 'al-Nass al-Muhaqqaq' (the authenticated text), then immediately gives the title. Find that line and note the exact wording.
The heading separates the corrupted manuscript colophon from the clean text.
2
Extract the full title
The title is 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq fi 'Ilm al-Mantiq'. Write it down exactly. 'Al-Sullam' means 'the ladder' and 'al-Murawnaq' means 'ornamented' or 'embellished'.
This title is a memorization anchor for the entire work.
3
Identify the author
The passage attributes the work to 'al-Akhḍarī'. Confirm that this is the author's name (a kunya or nisba).
Al-Akhḍarī's full name is usually given elsewhere as 'Abū Zayd 'Abd al-Raḥmān b. Muḥammad al-Akhḍarī'.
4
Understand the title's significance
The title 'The Ornamented Ladder' suggests a structured ascent in the science of logic. The metaphor of a ladder indicates that logic is a tool that elevates the student from simple concepts to complex demonstrations.
This metaphor is common in Islamic educational texts; for example, al-Akhḍarī's other work is 'al-Jawhar al-Maknūn' (the Hidden Jewel).

Author's Preface: The Purpose of Logic in Relation to Revelation
Al-Akhdari introduces his versified logic text after praise of knowledge and the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how al-Akhdari frames the relationship between rational argumentation (al-ma'qul) and transmitted revelation (al-manqul) in his preface.

Core Idea
Logic is a servant to revelation: rational proofs verify the transmitted.

Al-Akhdari opens his didactic poem on logic with a richly rhetorical preface that establishes the purpose of the entire discipline. He does not present logic as an independent speculative exercise; rather, he positions it within the broader Islamic project of verifying and understanding the revealed sources. This preface sets the epistemological stage for the entire Sullam al-Marwuq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening invocations
Locate the basmala and the hamdala. Note the praise of scholars whose hearts are illuminated by knowledge of the sciences. This is standard, but notice the specific imagery: hearts as heavens, intellects as gardens.
2
Find the pair al-ma'qul and al-manqul
In the description of scholars, find the phrase 'following the traces of the foundations (al-usul) seeking verification of the transmitted (al-manqul)' and note that they travel upon the carpet of rational proofs (hujaj al-ma'qul). This explicitly links rational inquiry to the verification of revelation.
3
Parse the author's transition (amma ba'd)
After the praise and testimonies, al-Akhdari says 'amma ba'd' and states that he composed an urjuza called al-Sullam al-Marwuq in the science of logic. This signals that the main content is about to begin.
4
Reflect on the genre choice
The term 'urjuza' indicates a mnemonic poem in rajaz meter. Why would the author choose this for a logic text? What does this suggest about the intended pedagogical method?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وعلى آله وصحبه الحمد لله الذي جعل قلوب العلماء سموات تتجلى فيها شموس المعارف، ووسع دوائر أفهامهم فأولجهم قباب المخدرات من عرائس المعاني واللطائف، وحباهم بحدائق العقول فتناولوا من ثمراتها فأصبحت آفاق قلوبهم مشرقة بسائر العلوم، ففاقوا من عداهم من الورى واستقروا على ذرى المجد وحلوا منابر العز بما سبق لهم في الكتاب المرقوم فتاهوا في رحاب العلم وعرصات الفهم على بساط حجج المعقول متبعين آثار الأصول طلباً لتحقيق المنقول فأصبحوا على بصيرة من الدين وفي أبهج السبل سالكين. وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له الرب الكريم الذي تقدس وتعالى عن أن يحاط برفيع مجده وعظيم جلاله وكبريائه، وأشهد أن سيدنا ومولانا وحبيبنا وشفيعنا وذخرنا محمداً عبده ورسوله قطب الجمال وتاج الكمال وديوان الشرف وبدر الترف خاتم رسله وأنبيائه وسيد أصفيائه وأزكى أوليائه صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه صلاة أرقى بها مراقي الإخلاص وأنال بها غاية الاختصاص. أما بعد : فلما وضعت الأرجوزة المسماة بالسلم المرونق في علم المنطق
In the name of Allah, the Most Merciful, the Especially Merciful. And may Allah pray upon our master Muhammad and upon his family and his companions. Praise be to Allah who made the hearts of scholars heavens in which the suns of knowledges shine, and He expanded the orbits of their understandings so He admitted them into the domed chambers of the brides of meanings and subtleties, and He gifted them with the gardens of intellects so they partook of their fruits, so the horizons of their hearts became radiant with all the sciences, so they surpassed those besides them from mortals and settled upon the summits of glory and descended upon pulpits of might with what preceded for them in the written Book, so they wandered in the vast spaces of knowledge and the open grounds of understanding upon the carpet of the arguments of the rational, following the traces of the foundations seeking verification of the transmitted, so they became upon insight in the religion and in the most delightful of paths traveling. And I testify that there is no god but Allah alone, no partner for Him, the Noble Lord who is sanctified and exalted above being encompassed by the exaltation of His glory and the greatness of His majesty and His magnificence. And I testify that our master and our protector and our beloved and our intercessor and our store Muhammad is His servant and His messenger, the pole of beauty and the crown of perfection and the register of honor and the full moon of elevation, the seal of His messengers and His prophets and the master of His chosen ones and the purest of His allies, may Allah pray upon him and upon his family and his companions a prayer by which the levels of sincerity are ascended and by which the ultimate distinction is attained. As for what follows: since I composed the urjuza named al-Sullam al-Marwuq in the science of logic
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is the author's prefatory dedication (dibaja) to his versified logic text. Al-Akhdari opens with the basmala, praise of God for elevating scholars through knowledge, and the shahada. He describes scholars as those whose hearts become heavens illuminated by the suns of knowledge, and whose minds are granted access to the chambers of subtle meanings and the gardens of intellect, enabling them to surpass others and attain the highest stations of glory through their mastery of the written revelation and rational proofs. He then testifies to God's transcendence and to the prophethood of Muhammad, describing him with lofty attributes and seeking elevation through prayer. Finally, he transitions to his purpose: stating that he has composed an urjuza—a didactic poem—entitled al-Sullam al-Marwuq in the science of logic. This establishes the genre and intent of the work: a concise, versified presentation of logic meant to be memorized and mastered, pursued alongside the transmitted sources to verify religious knowledge.

Key Terms
أرجوزة - urjuza - A didactic poem in rajaz meter; al-Akhdari uses this genre for his logic text.
المعقول - al-ma'qul - Rational arguments or intelligibles; here it means rational proofs.
المنقول - al-manqul - Transmitted knowledge, specifically the revealed texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Al-Akhdari states that he composed the Sullam al-Marwuq as an urjuza. What is the significance of this genre for studying logic?
A) It is a lengthy prose commentary requiring extensive parsing
B) It is a compressed didactic poem intended for memorization and mastery
C) It is a dialectical debate between multiple scholars
D) It is an autobiographical account of the author's teachers

Key Takeaway
Al-Akhdari's preface establishes logic not as an independent science but as a tool for verifying and understanding the transmitted revelation.

The author uses rich imagery to describe scholars. Identify one metaphor (e.g., 'hearts as heavens') and explain how it relates to the role of logic.

Author's Purpose in Composing the Commentary
Author explains why he composed this commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author’s stated purpose and intended audience for composing a sharh in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
A commentary is written not from superiority, but from optimism and service to beginners.

In this preface, the author of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq explains why he wrote this commentary. He reveals his humility and pedagogical intent, setting the tone for the work. Understanding this context helps you approach any commentary with the right expectations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the motivational trigger
Find what prompted the author to write the commentary. The text says some students repeatedly urged him to do so (رَاوَدَنِي بَعْضُ الإِخْوَانِ مِنَ الطُّلَّابِ الْمَرَّةَ بَعْدَ الْمَرَّةِ).
2
Identify the author's self-assessment
Does the author claim expertise or express modesty? He says 'لَسْتُ أَهْلاً لِذَلِكَ' (I am not worthy of that) and attributes his undertaking to 'تَفَاؤُلِي' (my optimism).
3
Identify the intended audience
For whom did he write the commentary? He states explicitly: 'لِأَمْثَالِي مِنَ الْمُبْتَدِئِينَ' (for my likes among beginners), not for those above him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بَعْدُ: فَلَمَّا وَضَعْتُ الأَرْجُوزَةَ الْمُسَمَّاةَ بِالسُّلَّمِ الْمُرَوَّنَقِ فِي عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ، وَجَاءَتْ بِحَمْدِ اللهِ جُمْلَةً كَافِيَةً وَلِمَقَاصِدَ مِنْ فَنِّهَا حَاوِيَةً، رَاوَدَنِي بَعْضُ الإِخْوَانِ مِنَ الطُّلَّابِ أَكْرَمَهُمُ اللهُ الْمَرَّةَ بَعْدَ الْمَرَّةِ عَلَى أَنْ أَضَعَ عَلَيْهَا شَرْحاً مُفِيداً يَبُثُّ مَا انْطَوَتْ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي وَيُشِيدُ مَا تَقَاصَرَ فِيهَا مِنَ الْمَبَانِي، فَأَجَبْتُهُمْ لِذَلِكَ طَالِباً مِنَ اللهِ تَعَالَى حُسْنَ التَّوْفِيقِ إِلَى مَهَائِعَ[؟] التَّحْقِيقِ، وَإِنْ كُنْتُ لَسْتُ أَهْلاً لِذَلِكَ وَلَكِنَّنِي حَمَلَنِي عَلَيْهِ تَفَاؤُلِي، وَلَمْ أَضَعْهُ لِمَنْ هُوَ أَعْلَى مِنِّي، بَلْ لِأَمْثَالِي مِنَ الْمُبْتَدِئِينَ. وَاللهِ اللهِ يَا أَخِي فِي الِاعْتِذَارِ وَتَرْكِ الِاعْتِرَاضِ، فَالْمُؤْمِنُ يَلْتَمِسُ الْعُذْرَ لِأَخِيهِ الْمُؤْمِنِ. وَاللهِ اللهِ فِي الدُّعَاءِ لِي وَلِوَالِدَيَّ بِالْمَغْفِرَةِ وَالرَّحْمَةِ.
As for what follows: when I composed the rajaz poem named al-Sullam al-Murawnaq in the science of logic, and it came, by the praise of Allah, as a sufficient whole and containing the purposes of its art, some of the brothers from the students—may Allah honor them—time after time urged me to place upon it a beneficial commentary that would spread what it contained of meanings and shore up what was abbreviated in it of structures. So I answered them in that, seeking from Allah, Exalted be He, the good success toward achievements[؟] of verification, though I am not worthy of that; rather, my optimism carried me to it. And I did not compose it for one who is above me, but for my likes among beginners. By Allah, by Allah, O my brother, in seeking excuse and leaving objection, for the believer seeks the excuse for his believing brother. By Allah, by Allah, in supplicating for me and for my parents for forgiveness and mercy.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a prefatory statement in which the author explains the occasion for composing his commentary. He begins by noting that after he composed the versified primer (al-Sullam al-Murawnaq) in logic, it was complete in itself and contained the aims of the art. Yet some student brothers repeatedly asked him to write a commentary that would unfold the condensed meanings and reinforce the abbreviated structures. He responded to their request, seeking Allah’s aid, while acknowledging his own inadequacy for the task; he attributes his undertaking to optimistic hope (tafa’ul) rather than to any claim of mastery. He further clarifies the intended audience: not advanced scholars, but beginners like himself. He closes with an earnest appeal to the reader to seek excuses for his shortcomings and to avoid censure, affirming that the believer seeks excuses for his brother, and he requests prayers for himself and his parents. The tone is characteristic of classical Islamic authorship: humility, invocation of divine aid, and a clear statement of pedagogical intent.

Key Terms
السُّلَّمُ الْمُرَوَّنَقُ (al-sullam al-murawnaq): The versified matn (rajaz poem) in logic upon which this commentary is written; described as 'al-murawnaq' meaning adorned or elegant. [Note: No significant shift — this is the specific title of al-Akhdari’s matn in this tradition.]
تَفَاؤُلِي (tafa’ulī): The author’s own hopeful optimism that carried him to undertake the commentary despite his felt unworthiness. [Note: Stable across adab and usul texts; here used to express authorial humility.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author compose this commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq?
A) Because advanced scholars demanded a refutation of the poem
B) Because students asked him, and he wrote it for beginners like himself
C) To replace the original poem with a prose summary
D) To demonstrate his superiority over other logicians

Key Takeaway
Al-Akhdari wrote this commentary to benefit beginners, motivated by student requests and his own optimism, not from any claim to mastery.

Examine the preface of another logical commentary, such as al-Qutb al-Tahtani's introduction to his commentary on al-Shamsiyya. Identify the author's stated purpose, intended audience, and any expressions of humility.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Corporeal and Psychic Veils of Knowledge
Author expounds the veiling of souls and illustrates it with poetic grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categories of veils that obstruct innate knowledge and explain how the Day of Alast serves as evidence for pre-veil cognition.

Core Idea
The soul's knowledge is veiled by bodily stages, not absent.

This passage from the prefatory section of the commentary introduces a foundational epistemological principle: the souls of the righteous possess innate knowledge that is obstructed by corporeal and psychic veils corresponding to the stages of bodily development. The author grounds this claim in scriptural evidence from the Day of Alast, and then traces how the body's growth stages become veils over the spirit. Understanding this metaphysical framework is essential for appreciating why the logical tools of the Sullam are needed to tear away these veils.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find the assertion about the souls of the righteous. What prevents them from perceiving? The passage states: 'nothing prevented them from that except the existence of the corporeal and psychic veils.'
2
Distinguish the two types of veils
Note the distinction: corporeal veils (jismāniyya) and psychic veils (nafsāniyya). They are 'according to the number of the stages' – the stages of bodily growth.
These veils are not permanent but accumulative.
3
Locate the scriptural proof
The author cites 'His affirmation in manifestation on the Day of 'Am I not your Lord?'' (Sūrat al-Aʿrāf 7:172). This is the Day of Alast, where spirits acknowledged God before embodiment.
This proves that knowledge existed before veils.
4
Explain the cosmological setting
The passage contrasts the Malakut (spiritual realm) and Mulk (worldly realm). The spirit is placed in the body, and the stages of the body become veils that obscure the spirit's innate knowledge.
The ignorance that arises is existential, not a lack of capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفوس الأخيار وإنما عاقها عن ذلك وجود الحجب الجسمانية والنفسانية التي على عدد الأطوار ويدلك على إدراك قبل الحجاب إقراره في الظهور، يوم ألستم بربكم بالوحدانية لانتفاء الحجاب الحائل بينه وبين الصواب وذلك أن الأرواح من العوالم الملكوتية والأبدان من العوالم الملكية، فوضع العالم الروحاني في القالب الجسماني ليتم الوعد الرباني فصارت أطوار البدن حجباً للروح فنسبت ما أدركته بسبب تلك الحجب فخوطبت بعد الظهور بما قرأت به في الظهور فتبين من هذا الجهل أمر وجودي وهو الناشيء عن الحجاب الحائل بين الروح والمعاني الدقيقة حتى صارت لا تدركها إلا بالتفكير وخرق الحجب العادية لمن وفقه الله تعالى وبه أستعين. حتى بدت لهم شموس المعرفة رأوا مخدراتها منكشفة هذا البيت من تمام ما قبله بينا فيه ثمرة رفع الحجاب عن قلوب أولي الألباب والمعنى حط عنهم ذلك حتى انتهى بهم الأمر إلى أن ظهرت لهم شموس من الأفهام والمعارف فنظروا مخدرات عرائس المعاني واللطائف، وقولنا: (رأوا مخدراتها) على حذف مضاف أي رأوا مخدرات عرائس المعرفة منكشفة وهذا النوع من المجاز الذي يعرف بلزوم تقييده كجناح الذل والخدر والستر، قال امرؤ القيس ويوم دخلت الخدر خدر عنيزة فقالت لك الويلات إنك مرجلي والضمير في قوله: (رأوا) عائد أيضاً لأرباب الحجا وهذا البيت نظير.
In the souls of the righteous, and nothing prevented them from that except the existence of the corporeal and psychic veils that are according to the number of the stages; and what indicates perception before the veil is His affirmation in manifestation on the Day of ‘Am I not your Lord?’ with Oneness, due to the absence of the intervening veil between it and the truth. And that is because spirits are of the Malakut realms and bodies are of the Mulk realms, so the spiritual world was placed in the bodily mold for the divine promise to be fulfilled, so the stages of the body became veils for the spirit, so what it perceived because of those veils was attributed, and it was addressed after manifestation with what it recited in manifestation. Thus an existential matter became clear from this ignorance, which is that arising from the intervening veil between the spirit and subtle meanings, until it became such that it does not perceive them except through reflection and tearing the habitual veils, for whom God granted success; and in Him I seek help. Until the suns of knowledge appeared to them: they saw their private chambers unveiled. This line, from the completion of what preceded it, we have explained in it the fruit of lifting the veil from the hearts of the people of reason; and the meaning is: that [veil] was lifted from them until the matter led them to the appearance of suns of understandings and knowledges, so they looked upon the private chambers of the brides of meanings and subtleties. And our statement: (they saw their private chambers) is upon the ellipsis of a genitive, meaning they saw the private chambers of the brides of knowledge unveiled; and this is a type of metaphor that is known by the necessity of its qualification, like the wing of humiliation and the private chamber and the cover. Imruʾ al-Qays said: And the day I entered the private chamber, the private chamber of Umayza, and she said to you: Woe unto you, for you are one who abandons me. And the pronoun in his statement: (they saw) also returns to the people of reason, and this line is similar [to it].
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and literary exposition on the veiling and unveiling of knowledge in the prefatory section of the work. The author begins with the metaphysical premise that within the souls of the righteous lies a knowledge which is obstructed only by corporeal and psychic veils, these veils being as numerous as the stages of bodily growth. He cites the Day of Alast as evidence for pre-veil cognition, noting that the spirit acknowledged divine lordship in a state of unveiled immediacy before the intervention of bodily density. He then draws a cosmological distinction between the Malakut, the unseen realm to which spirits belong, and the Mulk, the sensory realm to which bodies belong. The lodging of the spiritual substance within the bodily frame, he explains, fulfills a divine covenant, yet it entails that the successive stages of bodily growth become veils over the spirit, such that the soul eventually attributes its perceptions to the bodily instruments themselves and must be re-addressed after earthly manifestation with the very covenant it uttered in pre-existence. From this condition the author identifies an existential ignorance rooted not in the absence of capacity but in the obstruction of the intervening veil between the spirit and subtle intelligibles, an ignorance that can only be pierced through disciplined reflection and the tearing of habitual veils, which is granted to whom God chooses. He then turns to a poetic line describing the appearance of the suns of knowledge and the unveiling of private chambers, reading it as the fruit of this veil-lifting. He unpacks the grammar of the line: the phrase “their private chambers” operates upon the ellipsis of a genitive, so that the full expression is “the private chambers of the brides of knowledge,” which is a necessary qualification identifying this as a specific type of metaphor. He supports this literary analysis with a poetic witness from Imruʾ al-Qays, and he concludes by noting that the pronoun in “they saw” returns to the people of reason, affirming that this line stands in parallel to the preceding discourse.

Key Terms
الحجب الجسمانية والنفسانية (al-ḥujub al-jismāniyya wa-l-nafsāniyya): The bodily and psychic veils that obstruct the soul's innate knowledge according to the number of its stages. [Note: In formal Mantiq curricula these Sufi-cosmological terms are absent; they belong to the commentary’s prefatory metaphysics, not to the logical apparatus of the Sullam proper.]
يوم ألستم (yawm alastu): The Day of Covenant cited as scriptural proof that spirits possessed knowledge before embodiment and the intervention of veils. [Note: A Qurʾānic/Kalāmic reference (Sūrat al-Aʿrāf 7:172); it is not a technical term of Mantiq but an epistemological proof here.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that souls possessed knowledge before embodiment?
A) The testimony of the prophets
B) The Day of Alast covenant
C) The appearance of the suns of knowledge
D) The analysis of the poetic line

Key Takeaway
The soul's pre-veil knowledge, proven by the Day of Alast, is obstructed by corporeal and psychic veils corresponding to bodily stages, but can be regained through reflection and divine grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the concept of corporeal and psychic veils, analyze how the stages of human development (infancy, childhood, adulthood) correspond to increasing veils. Then explain how the Day of Alast provides evidence that the original state is knowledge, not ignorance.

Unveiling the Metaphor: Grammatical Ellipsis in Poetic Diction
Author expounds the veiling of souls and illustrates it with poetic grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize the ellipsis of the genitive (ḥadhf muḍāf) in a poetic metaphor and identify the type of majāz that requires an added qualification.

Core Idea
The metaphor of unveiled brides requires grammatical ellipsis to be understood correctly.

The passage concludes with a poetic line describing the suns of knowledge appearing and the private chambers of brides being unveiled. The author provides a grammatical and literary analysis, explaining that the phrase 'their private chambers' involves an ellipsis of the genitive (ḥadhf muḍāf) – the full expression is 'the private chambers of the brides of knowledge.' He further identifies this as a type of metaphor (majāz) that requires a necessary qualification. This lesson unpacks that analysis, using the example of Imruʾ al-Qays's poetry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the poetic line
Find the line: 'Until the suns of knowledge appeared to them: they saw their private chambers unveiled.' Note that it is cited as the fruit of veil-lifting.
2
Recognize the ellipsis
The author says 'they saw their private chambers' is on ḥadhf muḍāf. Supply the missing genitive: 'the private chambers of the brides of knowledge.' The 'brides' are the meanings/knowledge.
Ellipsis of the genitive is a common rhetorical device.
3
Understand the type of majāz
The author notes this is a type of metaphor known by 'the necessity of its qualification' (luzūm taqyīd). The qualification 'of knowledge' is necessary to make sense of the metaphor.
Similar to 'wing of humility' or 'private chamber of a bride.'
4
Connect to the poetic witness
The author quotes Imruʾ al-Qays: 'And the day I entered the private chamber, the private chamber of Umayza...' This shows the same construction with ellipsis.
The pronoun in 'they saw' refers to the people of reason (arbāb al-ḥijā).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفوس الأخيار وإنما عاقها عن ذلك وجود الحجب الجسمانية والنفسانية التي على عدد الأطوار ويدلك على إدراك قبل الحجاب إقراره في الظهور، يوم ألستم بربكم بالوحدانية لانتفاء الحجاب الحائل بينه وبين الصواب وذلك أن الأرواح من العوالم الملكوتية والأبدان من العوالم الملكية، فوضع العالم الروحاني في القالب الجسماني ليتم الوعد الرباني فصارت أطوار البدن حجباً للروح فنسبت ما أدركته بسبب تلك الحجب فخوطبت بعد الظهور بما قرأت به في الظهور فتبين من هذا الجهل أمر وجودي وهو الناشيء عن الحجاب الحائل بين الروح والمعاني الدقيقة حتى صارت لا تدركها إلا بالتفكير وخرق الحجب العادية لمن وفقه الله تعالى وبه أستعين. حتى بدت لهم شموس المعرفة رأوا مخدراتها منكشفة هذا البيت من تمام ما قبله بينا فيه ثمرة رفع الحجاب عن قلوب أولي الألباب والمعنى حط عنهم ذلك حتى انتهى بهم الأمر إلى أن ظهرت لهم شموس من الأفهام والمعارف فنظروا مخدرات عرائس المعاني واللطائف، وقولنا: (رأوا مخدراتها) على حذف مضاف أي رأوا مخدرات عرائس المعرفة منكشفة وهذا النوع من المجاز الذي يعرف بلزوم تقييده كجناح الذل والخدر والستر، قال امرؤ القيس ويوم دخلت الخدر خدر عنيزة فقالت لك الويلات إنك مرجلي والضمير في قوله: (رأوا) عائد أيضاً لأرباب الحجا وهذا البيت نظير.
In the souls of the righteous, and nothing prevented them from that except the existence of the corporeal and psychic veils that are according to the number of the stages; and what indicates perception before the veil is His affirmation in manifestation on the Day of ‘Am I not your Lord?’ with Oneness, due to the absence of the intervening veil between it and the truth. And that is because spirits are of the Malakut realms and bodies are of the Mulk realms, so the spiritual world was placed in the bodily mold for the divine promise to be fulfilled, so the stages of the body became veils for the spirit, so what it perceived because of those veils was attributed, and it was addressed after manifestation with what it recited in manifestation. Thus an existential matter became clear from this ignorance, which is that arising from the intervening veil between the spirit and subtle meanings, until it became such that it does not perceive them except through reflection and tearing the habitual veils, for whom God granted success; and in Him I seek help. Until the suns of knowledge appeared to them: they saw their private chambers unveiled. This line, from the completion of what preceded it, we have explained in it the fruit of lifting the veil from the hearts of the people of reason; and the meaning is: that [veil] was lifted from them until the matter led them to the appearance of suns of understandings and knowledges, so they looked upon the private chambers of the brides of meanings and subtleties. And our statement: (they saw their private chambers) is upon the ellipsis of a genitive, meaning they saw the private chambers of the brides of knowledge unveiled; and this is a type of metaphor that is known by the necessity of its qualification, like the wing of humiliation and the private chamber and the cover. Imruʾ al-Qays said: And the day I entered the private chamber, the private chamber of Umayza, and she said to you: Woe unto you, for you are one who abandons me. And the pronoun in his statement: (they saw) also returns to the people of reason, and this line is similar [to it].
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and literary exposition on the veiling and unveiling of knowledge in the prefatory section of the work. The author begins with the metaphysical premise that within the souls of the righteous lies a knowledge which is obstructed only by corporeal and psychic veils, these veils being as numerous as the stages of bodily growth. He cites the Day of Alast as evidence for pre-veil cognition, noting that the spirit acknowledged divine lordship in a state of unveiled immediacy before the intervention of bodily density. He then draws a cosmological distinction between the Malakut, the unseen realm to which spirits belong, and the Mulk, the sensory realm to which bodies belong. The lodging of the spiritual substance within the bodily frame, he explains, fulfills a divine covenant, yet it entails that the successive stages of bodily growth become veils over the spirit, such that the soul eventually attributes its perceptions to the bodily instruments themselves and must be re-addressed after earthly manifestation with the very covenant it uttered in pre-existence. From this condition the author identifies an existential ignorance rooted not in the absence of capacity but in the obstruction of the intervening veil between the spirit and subtle intelligibles, an ignorance that can only be pierced through disciplined reflection and the tearing of habitual veils, which is granted to whom God chooses. He then turns to a poetic line describing the appearance of the suns of knowledge and the unveiling of private chambers, reading it as the fruit of this veil-lifting. He unpacks the grammar of the line: the phrase “their private chambers” operates upon the ellipsis of a genitive, so that the full expression is “the private chambers of the brides of knowledge,” which is a necessary qualification identifying this as a specific type of metaphor. He supports this literary analysis with a poetic witness from Imruʾ al-Qays, and he concludes by noting that the pronoun in “they saw” returns to the people of reason, affirming that this line stands in parallel to the preceding discourse.

Key Terms
حذف مضاف (ḥadhf muḍāf): Ellipsis of the genitive noun (mudāf), where the possessive phrase is omitted and must be supplied by the reader; here “her private chambers” implies “the private chambers of the brides of knowledge.” [Note: A Nahw term here applied to Sufi poetic diction in a logic-book preface; in Mantiq proper ḥadhf usually refers to the ellipsis of a premise in a compound syllogism.]
مجاز (majāz): Figurative or metaphorical usage identified by the necessity of an added qualification (luzūm taqyīd) to make the meaning coherent. [Note: In Balāgha majāz is broad figurative speech; in Mantiq texts majāz can refer specifically to figurative predication in propositions, whereas here it is a literary metaphor.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the omitted word in the phrase 'their private chambers' according to the passage?
A) The suns
B) The brides
C) The veil
D) The knowledge

Key Takeaway
The ellipsis of the genitive (ḥadhf muḍāf) in poetic metaphors requires the reader to supply the missing element, and the necessity of qualification distinguishes this type of majāz.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the Qur'anic metaphor 'wing of humility' (janāḥ al-dhull) in Sūrat al-Isrā' 17:24. Identify the ellipsis (if any) and explain why it is a metaphor requiring qualification.

Grammar and Theology of Divine Praise
Author introduces his poem and analyzes the tense of divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the rhetorical function of verb tense in divine praise and identify the qualified nature of that praise as applied to the blessing of faith and Islam.

Core Idea
Present tense signals perpetual praise; praise is qualified by its object.

The author of al-Zahra al-Sunniyya opens his poem with an invocation that subtly communicates theological commitment through grammar. In this lesson, we examine his commentary on the verb 'naḥmiduhu' and the phrase 'jal' to see how a seemingly minor grammatical choice conveys the permanence of praise, and how the praise is restricted (muqayyad) to the greatest blessing: faith and Islam.

Guided Path
1
Isolate 'naḥmiduhu' and note its tense
The verb is in the present tense (muḍāriʿ). Recognize that the commentator explicitly highlights this choice.
In Arabic, the present tense often indicates ongoing or habitual action.
2
Contrast with past tense alternative
Consider what would change if the past (e.g., ḥamidnā) were used. The commentary states past suggests cessation, present suggests permanence.
This contrast is key to understanding the author's rhetorical intention.
3
Identify 'jal' and its meaning
The term 'jal' is parsed as meaning 'the Great' (ʿaẓīm). This qualifies the recipient of praise.
In Islamic theology, divine names like 'Jalīl' convey majesty.
4
Understand 'muqayyad' (qualified praise)
The praise is not absolute; it is specified by the phrase 'for the bestowal of the blessing of faith and Islam'.
This qualification ties the praise to a specific blessing, emphasizing its magnitude.
5
Connect to the poem's purpose
The poem aims to invoke God's blessing and teach doctrinal truths. The grammatical analysis reinforces gratitude for faith and Islam, and the supplication for a good end.
This shows how rhetorical choices serve theological ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا في الأرجوزة الموسومة بالزهرة السنية: فأصبحت شمس القلوب مشرقة وبجلال ربها محققة نحمده جل على الإنعام بنعمة الإيمان والإسلام عبر بالمضارع في نحمده دون الماضي إشعاراً بدوام الحمد واستمراره إذ هو مشعر بالثبوت والماضي بالانقطاع، وقوله: (على الإنعام) متعلق بنحمده وجلَّ بمعنى عظم والحمد هنا مقيد ولا شك أن من أجل النعم التي يجب أن يحمد عليها تبارك وتعالى نعمة الإيمان والإسلام إذ هي محل الفائدة ونجاة العائدة نسأله سبحانه وتعالى أن يختم لنا بأكمل حالات الإيمان والإسلام وبالله التوفيق.
Our statement regarding the poem entitled al-Zahra al-Sunniyya: The sun of hearts became shining and by the majesty of her Lord fulfilled; we praise Him, the Mighty, for the bestowal of the blessing of faith and Islam. He used the present tense in ‘we praise Him’ and not the past tense to indicate the permanence of praise and its continuance, since it indicates persistence and the past indicates cessation, and his statement: (for the bestowal) is connected to ‘we praise Him,’ and ‘the Mighty’ means ‘the Great,’ and the praise here is qualified; and there is no doubt that among the greatest blessings for which it is obligatory to praise Him, blessed and exalted is He, is the blessing of faith and Islam, since it is the locus of benefit and the salvation of the one who returns. We ask Him, glorified and exalted is He, to seal for us with the most complete states of faith and Islam; and in God is success.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage introduces the author’s poem al-Zahra al-Sunniyya and comments upon its opening line and theological grammar. He cites the line: “The sun of hearts became shining and by the majesty of her Lord fulfilled; we praise Him, the Mighty, for the bestowal of the blessing of faith and Islam.” The author begins his grammatical analysis with the verb “we praise Him,” noting that the commentator employed the present tense rather than the past tense to indicate the permanence and continuity of praise, since the present tense in Arabic conveys persistence while the past conveys termination. He then construes the phrase “for the bestowal” as grammatically connected to “we praise Him,” and explains “the Mighty” (jal) as meaning “the Great.” The praise, he adds, is not absolute but qualified, and among the most tremendous blessings that necessitate this praise is the blessing of faith and Islam, for it is the locus of worldly and otherworldly benefit and the salvation of the repentant servant. The passage concludes with a supplication that God seal the author’s life in the most complete states of faith and Islam, acknowledging that success lies solely with God.

Key Terms
المضارع (al-muḍāriʿ): The present-tense verb form chosen here over the past tense to signify that praise is perpetual and uninterrupted. [Note: A Nahw category; in Mantiq proper the muḍāriʿ is not discussed for its rhetorical duration but for its truth-value in propositions about future or habitual states.]
جل (jal): In this context a divine attribute glossed as meaning ‘the Great’ (ʿaẓīm), restricting the praise to the majesty of God. [Note: A name of God drawn from Qurʾānic usage; not a term of art in formal logic.]
مقيد (muqayyad): Qualified or restricted; here the praise is not absolute but specified by the object for which gratitude is given. [Note: In Mantiq al-taqyīd refers to the qualification of a universal proposition; here it is a theological-rhetorical qualification.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator use the present tense (al-muḍāriʿ) in 'naḥmiduhu' rather than the past tense?
A) Because the past tense is grammatically incorrect after the verb 'aṣbaḥat'
B) To indicate the permanence and continuity of praise
C) Because the poem’s meter requires a present-tense rhyme
D) To distinguish gratitude for creation from gratitude for the Law

Key Takeaway
The present tense in Arabic conveys continuity, and divine praise in the poem is intentionally cast in that tense to underscore its perpetual nature, while the praise is further specified as gratitude for the greatest blessing: faith and Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the Qurʾānic phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' in Q.1:2. Compare its nominal form (masdar) with a hypothetical verbal form using the present tense. What nuance does the masdar add in terms of permanence? (Hint: the absence of temporal inflection suggests timelessness.)
?
Find a hadith where the Prophet (ṣ) says 'al-ḥamdu lillāh alladhī ...' mentioning a specific blessing. Identify whether the praise is qualified (muqayyad) and discuss why the qualification matters for gratitude.

Grammatical Substitution (Badal) in Logical Definitions
Author parses the relative clause and badal honoring the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term functions as a badal (grammatical substitute) in a definition and explain its logical role in specifying a general term.

Core Idea
Badal is the grammatical mechanism that turns a general concept into a specific referent — the backbone of precise definition.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the name 'Muḥammad' is a badal (substitute) for the word 'best' in the preceding line. This grammatical relationship is not merely poetic; it mirrors how logical definitions use substitution to narrow down a genus to a species. Understanding badal helps you recognize when a specific term is being singled out from a general category, which is exactly what a complete definition (hadd) does.

Guided Path
1
Locate the general term being substituted
In the line, 'khayr' (best) is a general superlative. The commentator says 'Muḥammad' is a badal from 'khayr'. Find the word 'khayr' in the previous line of the poem.
The previous line is not fully quoted here, but the reference is clear.
2
Identify the specific substitute
The name 'Muḥammad' substitutes for 'khayr'. Note that the substitute must be exactly equivalent in reference—it is not a different entity but the same referent under a specific name.
In grammar, badal requires coreference.
3
Apply to logical definition
In a hadd (real definition), the differentia acts like a badal by specifying the genus. For example, in defining 'human' as 'rational animal', 'rational' substitutes the general 'animal' with a specific differentia.
This is the logical parallel to grammatical badal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من خصنا بخير من قد أرسلا وخير من حاز المقامات العلا هذا إقرار بنعمة أخرى من أعظم النعم التي يجب علينا أن نحمد الله تعالى عليها وهي أن جعلنا أمة سيد أهل السموات والأرض رئيس الأشراف وسلطان الموقف لا تسليماً كثيراً لأنه خيرة المرسلين وأمته خير الأمم قال تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ وقال: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا ومن قولنا: (من خصنا) موصولة خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي: هو الذي خصنا، ثم فسره باسمه الأعظم بقوله: محمد سيد كل مقتفى العربي الهاشمي المصطفى (محمد) بدل من لفظة خير في البيت المتقدم وسيد نعته (والمقتفى) المتبع والمراد به المرسلون ولا شك أنه [؟] وأشرف المرسلين لقوله: «أنا
Who singled us out with the best of those sent and the best of those who attained the highest stations: this is an affirmation of another blessing from the greatest blessings for which it is obligatory for us to praise God, exalted is He, which is that He made us the community of the master of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, the chief of the nobles and the sovereign of the Gathering, not excessive submission, for he is the best of those sent and his community is the best of communities. God, exalted is He, said: ‘You are the best community brought forth for mankind,’ and He said: ‘And thus We have made you a middle community.’ And [as for] our statement: (who singled us out), it is a relative clause [serving as] the predicate of an omitted mubtadaʾ, that is: ‘He is the One who singled us out’; then he explained him by His Greatest Name by his statement: ‘Muḥammad, master of every follower, the Arab, the Hāshimite, the Chosen One.’ (Muḥammad) is a substitute for the word ‘best’ in the preceding line, and ‘master’ is his adjective, and (al-muqtafā) is the tabiʿ; and the intended meaning by it is the messengers; and there is no doubt that he is [؟] and the noblest of the messengers, for his statement: ‘I am...’
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on al-Zahra al-Sunniyya with the line: “Who singled us out with the best of those sent and the best of those who attained the highest stations.” The author identifies this as an acknowledgment of a second tremendous blessing, namely that God made the Muslim community the followers of the master of all creation, the chief of the noble and the sovereign of the Resurrection. This favor, he insists, is not excessive flattery but corresponds to the Prophet’s objective preeminence as the best of messengers and his community’s status as the best of nations, adducing the Qurʾānic verses “You are the best community brought forth for mankind” and “Thus We have made you a middle nation.” The author then shifts to grammatical analysis: the phrase “who singled us out” is a relative clause functioning as the predicate of an omitted mubtadaʾ, which is to be understood as “He is the One who singled us out.” He then explains that the name “Muḥammad”—enumerated with the titles “master of every follower, the Arab, the Hāshimite, the Chosen One”—is a grammatical substitute (badal) for the word “best” in the previous line. The word “master” is its adjective, while “al-muqtafā” is the tabiʿ, here intended to mean the messengers. The author concludes by affirming the Prophet’s unrivaled nobility among the messengers, citing a hadīth that begins with “I am...” as scriptural evidence.

موصولة
نعته
المتبع

Grammatical substitution (badal) is a precise tool for specifying a general term, directly parallel to the logical differentia in a definition.

Relative Clauses (Mawṣūla) as Predicates in Logical Propositions
Author parses the relative clause and badal honoring the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize a relative clause functioning as a predicate in an Arabic proposition and translate it into a logical predication.

Core Idea
A relative clause as predicate is the Arabic way of saying 'He is the one who...' — a key to unpacking implied claims.

The commentator explains that the phrase 'man khaṣṣanā' (who singled us out) is a relative clause (mawṣūla) serving as the predicate of an omitted subject (mubtadaʾ). This construction is ubiquitous in Islamic texts, especially in theological statements about God. By learning to identify this pattern, you can convert elliptical Arabic into explicit logical propositions, a skill essential for analyzing arguments in Kalam and Tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Spot the omitted mubtada'
The predicate 'man khaṣṣanā' is a relative clause. In Arabic, a relative clause can act as predicate if the subject is implied. Here, the omitted mubtada' is 'huwa' (he), referring to God or the Prophet.
The commentator says: 'i.e., He is the One who singled us out.'
2
Recognize the relative clause as predicate
The clause 'man khaṣṣanā' has the form of a relative sentence. It is not a noun but a sentence acting as a predicate. This is a common rhetorical and logical device.
In logic, this is a singular proposition: the subject is the omitted entity, the predicate is a description.
3
Translate into a logical proposition
A proposition like 'Huwa alladhī khaṣṣanā' is logically equivalent to 'The one who singled us out is He.' More simply: 'He is the one who singled us out' is a categorical proposition with a definite description as predicate.
This structure is frequent in the Qur'an, e.g., 'Huwa alladhī khalaqa al-samāwāt wa al-arḍ.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
