beginner
5 min
The Continuity of Praise Through the Present Tense
Author explains the grammatical choice of present tense in the opening praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author chose the present tense for praising Allah and identify the theological implication of verb tense selection in Arabic.

Core Idea
The present tense in praise indicates perpetual, unbroken praise, unlike the past which implies cessation.

The author begins his poem with 'نحمده' (we praise) in the present tense, not the past. This is a deliberate grammatical choice that carries a deep theological message: praise of Allah should be continuous and never-ending. In this lesson, you will learn how a simple verb form can convey a profound spiritual truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb tense
Find the verb 'نحمده' in the Arabic. Is it in the past (ماضي) or present (مضارع) tense?
2
Contrast with the past tense
The author states that the present tense indicates 'دوام الحمد واستمراره' (continuity and perpetuity), while the past tense implies 'انقطاع' (cessation). Why would the past tense imply cessation?
3
Consider the context of praise
Praise of Allah is not limited to a single past event; it should be ongoing. How does the present tense better express this?
4
Apply to other contexts
Can you think of other situations where a present tense verb is used in Islamic supplications or invocations to express continuity?
This is a grammatical observation with theological weight; such choices are common in religious texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا في الأرجوزة الموسومة بالزهرة السنية : فأصبحت شمس القلوب مشرقة وبجلال ربها محققة نحمده جل على الإنعام بنعمة الإيمان والإسلام عبر بالمضارع في نحمده دون الماضي إشعاراً بدوام الحمد واستمراره إذ هو مشعر بالثبوت والماضي بالانقطاع ، وقوله : (على الإنعام) متعلق بنحمده و(جل) بمعنى عظم والحمد هنا مقيد ولا شك أن من أجل النعم التي يجب أن يحمد عليها تبارك وتعالى نعمة الإيمان والإسلام إذ هي محل الفائدة ونجاة العائدة نسأله سبحانه وتعالى أن يختم لنا بأكمل حالات الإيمان والإسلام وبالله التوفيق . من خصنا بخير من قد أرسلا وخير من حاز المقامات العلا هذا إقرار بنعمة أخرى من أعظم النعم التي يجب علينا أن نحمد الله تعالى عليها وهي أن جعلنا أمة سيد أهل السموات والأرض رئيس الأشراف وسلطان الموقف لا تسليماً كثيراً لأنه خيرة المرسلين وأمته خير الأمم قال تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ وقال : وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وسطا ومن قولنا : (من) خصنا موصولة خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي : هو الذي خصنا، ثم فسره باسمه الأعظم بقوله : محمد سید کل مقتفى العربي الهاشمي المصطفى (محمد) بدل من لفظة خير في البيت المتقدم و(سيد) نعته و(المقتفى) المتبع والمراد به المرسلون ولا شك أنه سيدهم وأشرف المرسلين لقوله : «أنا سيد ولد آدم ولا شك» وقوله : «انا العاقب» وتقديم العربي في البيت على الهاشمي من حسن الترتيب العقلي لأن بني هاشم نوع من العرب وتقديم الجنس على نوعه أولى ثم قال : المصطفى) أي من بني هاشم إشارة إلى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن الله اصطفى كنانة من ولد إسماعيل واصطفى قريشاً من كنانة واصطفى من قريش بني هاشم واصطفاني من بني هاشم فأنا خيار من خيار من خيار . صلى عليه الله ما دام الحجا يخوض من بحر المعاني لججا لما ذكرنا اسمه في البيت المتقدم وجب علينا أن نصلي عليه لأن من ذكره أو ذكر بين يديه ولم يصل عليه بخيل والصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واجبة على كل مسلم مرة في عمره وتبقى بعد ذلك مؤكدة قال الله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا وقال : «أولى الناس بي يوم القيامة أكثرهم علي صلاة» وقال : صلوا علي فإن صلاتكم تبلغني حيث كنتم
Our saying in the poem known as al-Zahra al-Sunniyya: 'So the sun of hearts has become shining and verified by the majesty of its Lord. We praise Him, Exalted, for the favor of the blessing of faith and Islam.' He used the present tense in 'we praise' instead of the past tense to indicate the continuity and perpetuity of praise, for it indicates constancy while the past indicates cessation. And His saying: 'for the favor' is attached to 'we praise', and 'Exalted' means 'Mighty'. And praise here is restricted. There is no doubt that among the greatest favors for which He, Blessed and Exalted, should be praised is the blessing of faith and Islam, for they are the place of benefit and the salvation of the return. We ask Him, Glorified and Exalted, to seal for us with the most complete states of faith and Islam, and with Allah is success. 'Who has singled us out with the best of those who were sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations.' This is an acknowledgment of another favor among the greatest favors for which we must praise Allah Almighty, namely that He made us the community of the master of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, the chief of the nobles, and the sultan of the standing place (of Judgment) — many salutations — because he is the best of the messengers and his community is the best of communities. Allah said: 'You are the best community brought forth for mankind' and said: 'And thus We have made you a middle community.' And from our saying: 'Who singled us out' is a relative clause, the predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., 'He is the one who singled us out.' Then he explained it with his greatest name by saying: 'Muhammad, the master of every follower, the Arab, the Hashimi, the chosen one.' 'Muhammad' is an appositive of 'best' in the preceding line, and 'master' is its attribute, and 'the follower' means 'the one followed', and by it is meant the messengers. There is no doubt that he is their master and the most honored of the messengers due to his saying: 'I am the master of the children of Adam, no doubt' and his saying: 'I am al-‘Aqib.' And the precedence of 'Arabi' over 'Hashimi' in the line is due to good rational ordering because Banu Hashim are a type of Arabs, and precedence of genus over its species is more proper. Then he said: 'al-Mustafa' i.e., from Banu Hashim, indicating his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah chose Kinanah from the children of Isma‘il, and chose Quraysh from Kinanah, and chose Banu Hashim from Quraysh, and chose me from Banu Hashim, so I am the best of the best of the best.' May Allah bless him as long as the mind wades into the depths of the sea of meanings. When we mentioned his name in the preceding line, it became obligatory upon us to send blessings upon him, because whoever mentions him or he is mentioned in his presence and does not send blessings upon him is miserly. And sending blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, is obligatory upon every Muslim once in his lifetime, and remains after that recommended. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and salute him with a worthy salutation.' And he said: 'The people most worthy of me on the Day of Resurrection are those who send the most blessings upon me.' And he said: 'Send blessings upon me, for your blessings reach me wherever you are.'
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the opening commentary by the author on his own didactic poem (urjuza) titled 'al-Zahra al-Sunniyya'. The author begins by quoting the first line of the poem: 'So the sun of hearts has become shining and verified by the majesty of its Lord. We praise Him, Exalted, for the favor of the blessing of faith and Islam.' He then explains his choice of the present tense 'نحمده' (we praise) instead of the past tense, noting that the present tense indicates continuity and constancy, while the past tense implies cessation. This is a grammatical point with theological significance: praise of Allah should be perpetual.

Next, he comments on the phrase 'على الإنعام' (for the favor), clarifying its syntactic attachment to the verb 'نحمده'. He then states that among the greatest favors for which Allah deserves praise is the blessing of faith (iman) and Islam, as they bring benefit and salvation. He prays for a good end.

The author then moves to the next line of the poem: 'Who has singled us out with the best of those who were sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations.' He explains that this acknowledges another great favor: being made the community (umma) of the Prophet Muhammad, who is the master of all creation, both in the heavens and on earth. He supports this by citing Quranic verses that refer to the Muslim community as the best community and a middle community.

He then parses the grammar of the relative clause 'من خصنا' (who singled us out), noting that it is a relative clause serving as the predicate of an implied subject. The author then glosses the names and descriptions of the Prophet in the poem: 'Muhammad, the master of every follower, the Arab, the Hashimi, the chosen one.' He explains that 'Muhammad' is an appositive to 'خير' (best), and 'master' is an attribute. The term 'المقتفى' (the one followed) refers to the messengers, and the Prophet is their master. He justifies the order 'العربي الهاشمي' (the Arab, the Hashimi) as a logical precedence of genus over species.

Finally, the author discusses the obligation to send blessings upon the Prophet whenever his name is mentioned. He cites the Qur'anic command and several hadiths indicating the virtue and obligation of sending salawat. He notes that it is obligatory once in a lifetime and thereafter recommended.

Key Terms
الحمد
المضارع
دوام الحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the present tense for 'نحمده' instead of the past tense?
A) To indicate that the praise is limited to a specific occasion.
B) To indicate the continuity and perpetuity of praise.
C) Because the past tense is grammatically incorrect in this context.
D) To rhyme with the following words.

Key Takeaway
The present tense verb 'نحمده' is chosen to express that praise of Allah is perpetual, not limited to a past event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Find a verse in the Qur'an where Allah uses the present tense (مضارع) for an action that is continuous. Explain why the present tense is appropriate there. Example: 'يُسَبِّحُ لِلَّهِ مَا فِي السَّمَاوَاتِ' (Everything in the heavens glorifies Allah).
?
2. In the poem, the author says 'نحمده' not 'حمدناه'. Compare the meanings of these two forms and state which one better suits the context of praising Allah.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Greatest Blessings: Faith, Islam, and Being the Ummah of the Prophet
Author enumerates the greatest blessings: faith, Islam, and being the ummah of the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two greatest favors mentioned by the author and explain why they are considered the most praiseworthy.

Core Idea
The greatest favors for which we must praise Allah are faith (iman) and Islam, and being made part of the Prophet's community.

The author states that among the greatest favors for which Allah should be praised are the blessings of faith and Islam. He then adds another favor: being made the community of the Prophet Muhammad, the master of all creation. In this lesson, we will explore why these are considered the highest blessings and how they are connected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first favor
The author says 'نعمة الإيمان والإسلام' (the blessing of faith and Islam). Why are these considered the greatest favors?
2
Understand the second favor
The author then says 'من خصنا بخير من قد أرسلا' (Who singled us out with the best of those sent). What is this favor? Who is the 'best of those sent'?
3
Connect the favors
How are these two favors related? Faith and Islam are the means to benefit from the Prophet's message.
4
Support from Qur'an
The author cites Qur'anic verses about the ummah being the best community. Why does he use these verses?
The author prays for a good end with the most complete states of faith and Islam.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Obligation and Virtue of Sending Blessings (Salawat) on the Prophet
Author discusses the ruling of sending blessings on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sending salawat upon the Prophet based on the author's explanation and provide evidence from the Qur'an and hadith.

Core Idea
Sending blessings on the Prophet is obligatory once in a lifetime and constantly recommended, with great virtue.

After mentioning the Prophet's name, the author asserts that it becomes obligatory to send blessings upon him. He cites the Qur'anic command and several hadiths to support this. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The author says sending salawat is 'واجبة على كل مسلم مرة في عمره' (obligatory upon every Muslim once in his lifetime). What does this mean?
2
Distinguish obligatory from recommended
After the first time, it remains 'مؤكدة' (emphasized). What is the difference?
3
Examine the evidence
The author quotes Qur'an 33:56 and hadith about 'أولى الناس بي يوم القيامة أكثرهم علي صلاة'. What do these texts teach?
4
Understand the reason
The author says whoever mentions the Prophet without sending blessings is 'بخيل' (miserly). Why is that?

قولنا في الأرجوزة الموسومة بالزهرة السنية : فأصبحت شمس القلوب مشرقة وبجلال ربها محققة نحمده جل على الإنعام بنعمة الإيمان والإسلام عبر بالمضارع في نحمده دون الماضي إشعاراً بدوام الحمد واستمراره إذ هو مشعر بالثبوت والماضي بالانقطاع ، وقوله : (على الإنعام) متعلق بنحمده و(جل) بمعنى عظم والحمد هنا مقيد ولا شك أن من أجل النعم التي يجب أن يحمد عليها تبارك وتعالى نعمة الإيمان والإسلام إذ هي محل الفائدة ونجاة العائدة نسأله سبحانه وتعالى أن يختم لنا بأكمل حالات الإيمان والإسلام وبالله التوفيق . من خصنا بخير من قد أرسلا وخير من حاز المقامات العلا هذا إقرار بنعمة أخرى من أعظم النعم التي يجب علينا أن نحمد الله تعالى عليها وهي أن جعلنا أمة سيد أهل السموات والأرض رئيس الأشراف وسلطان الموقف لا تسليماً كثيراً لأنه خيرة المرسلين وأمته خير الأمم قال تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ وقال : وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وسطا ومن قولنا : (من) خصنا موصولة خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي : هو الذي خصنا، ثم فسره باسمه الأعظم بقوله : محمد سید کل مقتفى العربي الهاشمي المصطفى (محمد) بدل من لفظة خير في البيت المتقدم و(سيد) نعته و(المقتفى) المتبع والمراد به المرسلون ولا شك أنه سيدهم وأشرف المرسلين لقوله : «أنا سيد ولد آدم ولا شك» وقوله : «انا العاقب» وتقديم العربي في البيت على الهاشمي من حسن الترتيب العقلي لأن بني هاشم نوع من العرب وتقديم الجنس على نوعه أولى ثم قال : المصطفى) أي من بني هاشم إشارة إلى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن الله اصطفى كنانة من ولد إسماعيل واصطفى قريشاً من كنانة واصطفى من قريش بني هاشم واصطفاني من بني هاشم فأنا خيار من خيار من خيار . صلى عليه الله ما دام الحجا يخوض من بحر المعاني لججا لما ذكرنا اسمه في البيت المتقدم وجب علينا أن نصلي عليه لأن من ذكره أو ذكر بين يديه ولم يصل عليه بخيل والصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واجبة على كل مسلم مرة في عمره وتبقى بعد ذلك مؤكدة قال الله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا وقال : «أولى الناس بي يوم القيامة أكثرهم علي صلاة» وقال : صلوا علي فإن صلاتكم تبلغني حيث كنتم
Our saying in the poem known as al-Zahra al-Sunniyya: 'So the sun of hearts has become shining and verified by the majesty of its Lord. We praise Him, Exalted, for the favor of the blessing of faith and Islam.' He used the present tense in 'we praise' instead of the past tense to indicate the continuity and perpetuity of praise, for it indicates constancy while the past indicates cessation. And His saying: 'for the favor' is attached to 'we praise', and 'Exalted' means 'Mighty'. And praise here is restricted. There is no doubt that among the greatest favors for which He, Blessed and Exalted, should be praised is the blessing of faith and Islam, for they are the place of benefit and the salvation of the return. We ask Him, Glorified and Exalted, to seal for us with the most complete states of faith and Islam, and with Allah is success. 'Who has singled us out with the best of those who were sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations.' This is an acknowledgment of another favor among the greatest favors for which we must praise Allah Almighty, namely that He made us the community of the master of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth, the chief of the nobles, and the sultan of the standing place (of Judgment) — many salutations — because he is the best of the messengers and his community is the best of communities. Allah said: 'You are the best community brought forth for mankind' and said: 'And thus We have made you a middle community.' And from our saying: 'Who singled us out' is a relative clause, the predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., 'He is the one who singled us out.' Then he explained it with his greatest name by saying: 'Muhammad, the master of every follower, the Arab, the Hashimi, the chosen one.' 'Muhammad' is an appositive of 'best' in the preceding line, and 'master' is its attribute, and 'the follower' means 'the one followed', and by it is meant the messengers. There is no doubt that he is their master and the most honored of the messengers due to his saying: 'I am the master of the children of Adam, no doubt' and his saying: 'I am al-‘Aqib.' And the precedence of 'Arabi' over 'Hashimi' in the line is due to good rational ordering because Banu Hashim are a type of Arabs, and precedence of genus over its species is more proper. Then he said: 'al-Mustafa' i.e., from Banu Hashim, indicating his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah chose Kinanah from the children of Isma‘il, and chose Quraysh from Kinanah, and chose Banu Hashim from Quraysh, and chose me from Banu Hashim, so I am the best of the best of the best.' May Allah bless him as long as the mind wades into the depths of the sea of meanings. When we mentioned his name in the preceding line, it became obligatory upon us to send blessings upon him, because whoever mentions him or he is mentioned in his presence and does not send blessings upon him is miserly. And sending blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, is obligatory upon every Muslim once in his lifetime, and remains after that recommended. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and salute him with a worthy salutation.' And he said: 'The people most worthy of me on the Day of Resurrection are those who send the most blessings upon me.' And he said: 'Send blessings upon me, for your blessings reach me wherever you are.'
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the opening commentary by the author on his own didactic poem (urjuza) titled 'al-Zahra al-Sunniyya'. The author begins by quoting the first line of the poem: 'So the sun of hearts has become shining and verified by the majesty of its Lord. We praise Him, Exalted, for the favor of the blessing of faith and Islam.' He then explains his choice of the present tense 'نحمده' (we praise) instead of the past tense, noting that the present tense indicates continuity and constancy, while the past tense implies cessation. This is a grammatical point with theological significance: praise of Allah should be perpetual.

Next, he comments on the phrase 'على الإنعام' (for the favor), clarifying its syntactic attachment to the verb 'نحمده'. He then states that among the greatest favors for which Allah deserves praise is the blessing of faith (iman) and Islam, as they bring benefit and salvation. He prays for a good end.

The author then moves to the next line of the poem: 'Who has singled us out with the best of those who were sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations.' He explains that this acknowledges another great favor: being made the community (umma) of the Prophet Muhammad, who is the master of all creation, both in the heavens and on earth. He supports this by citing Quranic verses that refer to the Muslim community as the best community and a middle community.

He then parses the grammar of the relative clause 'من خصنا' (who singled us out), noting that it is a relative clause serving as the predicate of an implied subject. The author then glosses the names and descriptions of the Prophet in the poem: 'Muhammad, the master of every follower, the Arab, the Hashimi, the chosen one.' He explains that 'Muhammad' is an appositive to 'خير' (best), and 'master' is an attribute. The term 'المقتفى' (the one followed) refers to the messengers, and the Prophet is their master. He justifies the order 'العربي الهاشمي' (the Arab, the Hashimi) as a logical precedence of genus over species.

Finally, the author discusses the obligation to send blessings upon the Prophet whenever his name is mentioned. He cites the Qur'anic command and several hadiths indicating the virtue and obligation of sending salawat. He notes that it is obligatory once in a lifetime and thereafter recommended.

Key Terms

واجب
مؤكدة

Genus and Species in Poetic Arrangement
Commentator explains poetic word order via genus-species logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical principle of genus-before-species in Arabic literary compositions and explain its significance.

Core Idea
The poet's word order follows logical hierarchy: genus before species is sound conceptual arrangement.

This passage from a commentary on a praise poem reveals that the poet's choice to mention 'Arab' before 'Hashimi' is not arbitrary. The commentator explicitly invokes the logical principle of genus preceding species (tartib 'aqli). This lesson shows how the five universals framework is not confined to formal definitions but governs the very order of speech in the highest literary productions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in the poem's line
Locate 'al-Arabi' and 'al-Hashimi' in the passage. These are the two terms whose order is being analyzed.
2
Determine their logical relationship
The commentator states that Banu Hashim are a type (naw') of Arabs. Thus, 'Arabs' is the genus (jins) and 'Banu Hashim' is the species.
In the five universals, a species falls under a genus.
3
Apply the rule of tartib 'aqli
The commentator says that placing the genus before the species is 'good logical arrangement' (husn al-tartib al-'aqli). This is the principle being demonstrated.
Tartib 'aqli means ordering concepts according to their natural logical hierarchy.
4
Connect to the hadith of divine selection
The hadith traces Allah's selection from Kinana to Quraysh to Banu Hashim to the Prophet. This mirrors the logical order from broader to narrower, reinforcing the same hierarchy.
The hadith illustrates the same logical principle in a sacred context.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
This shows that mantiq provides the rational framework even for literary expression. The order is not arbitrary but reflects conceptual soundness.
Islamic poetry and prose often reflect logical structures from the sciences of mantiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«سيد ولد آدم ولا شك» (۱) وقوله : «أنا العاقب» (۲) وتقديم العربي في البيت على الهاشمي من حسن الترتيب العقلي لأن بني هاشم نوع من العرب وتقديم الجنس على نوعه أولى ثم قال : (المصطفى) أي من بني هاشم إشارة إلى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إن الله اصطفى كنانة من ولد إسماعيل واصطفى قريشاً من كنانة واصطفى من قريش بني هاشم واصطفاني من بني هاشم فأنا خيار من خيار من خيار» (۳).
«Master of the children of Adam without doubt» (1), and his saying: «I am the last» (2). And putting the Arab in the line before the Hashimi is from good logical arrangement because Banu Hashim are a type of the Arabs, and putting the genus before its species is more appropriate. Then he said: (The Chosen One), meaning from Banu Hashim, an allusion to his statement, peace and blessings of Allah be upon him: «Indeed Allah chose Kinana from the children of Isma'il, and chose Quraysh from Kinana, and chose from Quraysh Banu Hashim, and chose me from Banu Hashim, so I am the best of the best of the best» (3).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage explains the arrangement of a praise poem using a logical principle drawn from the science of universals. The commentator begins by citing the poem's descriptions of the Prophet as master of Adam's children and the seal of prophecy, then turns to the poet's ordering of words. He notes that the poet placed al-Arabi before al-Hashimi and explains this as sound logical arrangement, appealing to the rule that the genus is mentioned before its species. Since Banu Hashim are a specific type under the general category of Arabs, mentioning the general before the specific follows the natural conceptual order. He then moves to the next line of the poem, al-Mustafa, glossing it as a reference to the Prophet's noble lineage from Banu Hashim and citing the well-known hadith of successive divine selection from Kinana to Quraysh to Banu Hashim to the Prophet himself, culminating in the phrase 'the best of the best of the best.' The passage thus joins literary exegesis with a technical logical concept from mantiq.

The Obligation and Unique Status of Salawat
Author establishes obligation of salawat with Quranic and prophetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal ruling of sending blessings upon the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) and explain why it is uniquely accepted among all good deeds, using the evidence from the Qur'an and hadith presented by the commentator.

Core Idea
Salawat upon the Prophet is obligatory once in a lifetime and then emphasized, and it is the only deed guaranteed acceptance.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a line of poetry mentioning the Prophet's name, establishing the obligation to send blessings and highlighting its unparalleled status. We will examine the Qur'anic command, the prophetic hadiths, and the logical distinction that while other deeds may be rejected, salawat is always accepted as an honor to the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Look at the sentence: 'الصلاة على النبي واجبة على كل مسلم مرة في عمره وتبقى بعد ذلك مؤكدة'. Determine the legal status: obligatory once in a lifetime and then emphasized.
2
Extract the evidence
Find the Qur'anic verse (Surat al-Ahzab) and the hadiths cited. Which ones support the obligation? Note the hadiths about closeness on the Day of Resurrection, reaching of blessings, light, and Friday.
3
Analyze the unique status
Read: 'واتفق على أن الأعمال مقبول ومردود إلا الصلاة عليه جميعًا فإنها مقطوع بقبولها'. Understand that while other deeds may be accepted or rejected, salawat is decisively accepted. Reflect on why this is the case.
4
Connect to the poet's context
Why does the commentator say that mentioning the Prophet in the preceding line makes it obligatory to send blessings? Because whoever hears his name and does not bless him is miserly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صلى عليه الله ما دام الحجا يخوض من بحر المعاني لججا لما ذكرنا اسمه في البيت المتقدم وجب علينا أن نصلي عليه لأن من ذكره أو ذكر بين يديه ولم يصل عليه بخيل والصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واجبة على كل مسلم مرة في عمره وتبقى بعد ذلك مؤكدة قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا﴾ (٤) وقال : «أولى الناس بي يوم القيامة أكثرهم علي صلاة» (٥) ، وقال : «صلوا علي فإن صلاتكم تبلغني حيث كنتم» (٦) ، وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «الصلاة علي نور يوم القيامة ونور في القلب ونور في القبر ونور على الصراط» (۱) ، وقال : «إن من أفضل أيامكم يوم الجمعة فأكثروا علي من الصلاة فيه» (۲). والأحاديث في فضلها جمة لا تنحصر وخصائصها لا تنضبط، فمن ذلك قضاء الحاجات وكشف الكرب المعضلات ونزول الرحمة في جميع الأوقات واتفق على أن الأعمال مقبول ومردود إلا الصلاة عليه جميعًا فإنها مقطوع بقبولها إكرامًا له عليه الصلاة والسلام وورد أن كل دعاء مفتتح ومختتم بها لا يرد وناهيك بهذا شرفًا وكفى به تفضيلاً والصلاة من الله تعالى زيادة تشريف وإكرام ورفع درجة وإنعام ومن الملائكة تسبيح ومنا دعاء
May Allah send blessings upon him as long as discourse plunges from the sea of meanings with depths. Since we mentioned his name in the preceding line, it is obligatory upon us to send blessings upon him, for whoever mentions him or is mentioned before him and does not send blessings upon him is miserly. Sending blessings upon the Prophet, peace and blessings of Allah be upon him, is obligatory upon every Muslim once in his lifetime, and after that it remains emphasized. Allah the Exalted said: «Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet; O you who believe, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace» (4). And he said: «Those closest to me on the Day of Resurrection are the most prolific in sending blessings upon me» (5). And he said: «Send blessings upon me, for your blessings reach me wherever you are» (6). And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: «Blessings upon me are light on the Day of Resurrection, light in the heart, light in the grave, and light upon the Sirat» (1). And he said: «Indeed the best of your days is Friday, so increase in sending blessings upon me on it» (2). The hadiths on its virtues are innumerable and its special qualities cannot be delimited, among which are: fulfillment of needs, relief of dire distress, and descent of mercy at all times. It is agreed that actions are accepted and rejected except blessings upon him entirely, for it is decisively accepted as an honour to him, peace and blessings be upon him. And it is reported that every supplication begun and concluded with it is not rejected. How far above comparison is this in nobility, and sufficient is it in preference. Blessings from Allah the Exalted are increased honour, reverence, raising of degree, and bestowal of favour; from the angels they are glorification; and from us they are supplication.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage establishes the legal and spiritual rationale for the line of poetry invoking blessings upon the Prophet. Because the poet mentioned the Prophet's name in the previous line, the commentator holds that it becomes obligatory to follow that mention with a prayer of blessings, citing the rule that whoever mentions the Prophet or hears him mentioned and withholds blessings is miserly. He distinguishes between the once-in-a-lifetime obligation incumbent upon every Muslim and the emphasized status of subsequent blessings. He then adduces the Qur'anic command from Surat al-Ahzab, followed by a series of prophetic hadiths: the ranking of people by their volume of blessings on the Day of Resurrection, the reaching of blessings to the Prophet wherever the supplicant may be, the light-bestowing quality of blessings in the grave, heart, Sirat, and Resurrection, and the especial merit of Friday for this practice. He enumerates fruits of the prayer—relief of calamity, answering of needs, and mercy—then notes the unique status of blessings among works: while other deeds may be accepted or rejected, blessings upon the Prophet are decisively accepted in their entirety as an honour to him. He concludes by describing the composition of the blessing: from Allah it is honour and elevation, from angels glorification, and from human beings supplication.

مؤكدة
بخيل

Key Takeaway

Grammar and Theological Inference from Poetic Imagery
Commentator parses the poem's grammar and points to human epistemic limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a grammatical construction in an Islamic poem and identify the theological inference drawn from the figurative language.

Core Idea
Figurative language about the sea of meaning reveals human epistemic limits — only Allah encompasses all knowledge.

This passage from the commentary on the Sullam al-Mantiq poem demonstrates how a seemingly poetic flourish carries deep theological substance. The commentator first resolves the grammar of the verse, then uses the imagery of an unfathomable sea to point to a foundational truth: no created being can fully encompass the divine knowledge. Understanding this move shows how Islamic scholars integrated linguistic analysis with kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical construction
The commentator glosses 'ma dama al-hija' as a verbal noun in an adverbial sense (masdariyya zarfiyya), meaning 'for the duration of discourse'. Note how he parses 'al-hija' (speech) to denote a period, not the act itself.
2
Unpack the metaphorical image
The phrase 'yakhudhu min bahr al-ma'ani lujjajan' literally means 'he plunges from the sea of meanings into depths'. The word 'lujj' (plural of 'luja') means deep pool or abyss. This suggests layers upon layers of meaning, like an ocean with unfathomable depths.
Notice the shift from concrete (sea) to abstract (knowledge).
3
Extract the theological inference
The commentator explicitly states: 'In this is a pointer that none encompasses the meanings except Allah.' He supports this with three Qur'anic verses: Q. 2:255, Q. 12:76, and Q. 20:114. Observe how the poetic image serves as a premise for a kalam argument about the limits of human knowledge.
The inference moves from 'the sea has unreachable depths' to 'only Allah knows all'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما من قولنا : ما دام (الحجا مصدرية ظرفية أي مدة دوام الحجا) (يخوض من بحر المعاني) (لججا) واللجج جمع لجة وهي البركة وفي هذا تنبيه على أنه لا يحتوي المعاني إلا الله تعالى كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِّنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ﴾ (۳)، وقال: ﴿وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ (٤)، وقال: ﴿وَقُلْ رَبِّ زِدْنِي عِلْمًا﴾ () وهذا البيت من تمام البراعة المذكورة في أول بيت، وبالله تعالى على التوفيق.
And regarding our saying 'as long as': al-hija is a verbal noun used adverbially, meaning the duration of the continuance of discourse; (he plunges from the sea of meanings); (depths); and al-lujj is the plural of luja, which is the pool. In this is a pointer that none encompasses the meanings except Allah the Exalted, as Allah the Exalted said: «And they do not encompass anything of His knowledge except what He wills» (3), and He said: «And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing» (4), and He said: «And say: My Lord, increase me in knowledge» (). This line is from the completion of the eloquence mentioned in the first line, and with Allah the Exalted is success.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical and semantic exposition of the poet's line invoking blessings upon the Prophet. The commentator parses the phrase 'ma dama' as an adverbial construction denoting duration, then glosses 'al-hija' as discourse or speech, explaining the imagery of plunging into a sea whose depths—the plural of luja, meaning deep pools—signify the unfathomable store of meanings. He draws a theological inference from this figurative language: just as the ocean of meaning has depths beyond human reach, so too no one truly encompasses knowledge except Allah. He supports this with three Qur'anic citations: the verse of the Footstool declaring the limits of created knowledge, the verse affirming that above every knower is a higher knower, and the supplication for increase in knowledge. He concludes by noting that this line completes the artistic eloquence established in the poem's opening, and closes with a formula of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms
مصدرية ظرفية
لججا
البراعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What theological point does the commentator derive from the image of 'the sea of meanings with its depths'?
A) That poets must learn swimming to understand theology
B) That the depths of meaning are unreachable and only Allah fully encompasses knowledge
C) That the Prophet alone knows the depths of all meanings
D) That grammar is deeper than the ocean

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that poetic imagery, when properly parsed, can serve as a vehicle for precise theological claims about the limits of human knowledge relative to Allah's infinitude.

The Ibrahimic Prayer: What the Hadith Teaches
Commentator links the poem's closing to the Ibrahimic prayer hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the precise wording of the salawat as taught by the Prophet and distinguish it from the greeting of peace.

Core Idea
The Prophet taught a specific formula pairing his family with Ibrahim's.

In this passage, the commentator links the poem's closing to a famous hadith where the Companions ask how to send blessings. The Prophet instructs them with the Ibrahimic formula. This lesson unpacks that hadith to clarify the exact wording and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Companions' question
The Companions say they already know the greeting of peace (as-salam) but need to know how to invoke blessings (as-salat) upon the Prophet.
This shows the two distinct forms of invocation.
2
Extract the Prophet's command
The Prophet teaches a specific formula: 'Say: O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You sent blessings upon Ibrahim...'
The formula is a prayer, not a greeting.
3
Note the parallel structure with Ibrahim
The blessing upon Muhammad and his family is modeled on the blessing upon Ibrahim and his family, indicating a supreme rank.
This emphasizes the exalted status of the Prophet's family.
4
Recognize the inclusion of the family
The phrase 'wa 'ala ali Muhammad' is obligatory, not optional, per this hadith.
Omitting the family would contradict the sunna.
5
Observe the closing divine names
The formula ends with 'innaka Hamidun Majid' (You are Praiseworthy, Majestic), reinforcing the proper adab.
These names are part of the taught wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وآله وصحبه ذوي الهدى من شبهوا بأنجم في الاهتدا ورد في الحديث أنهم قالوا أما السلام عليك عرفناه، فكيف نصلي عليك؟ فقال: «قولوا : اللهم صلِّ على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد وبارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد» (٦)،
And his family and his companions, possessors of guidance, who are likened to stars in guidance. It is reported in the hadith that they said: 'As for the greeting of peace upon you, we know it; so how do we send blessings upon you?' So he said: 'Say: O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad as You sent blessings upon Ibrahim and upon the family of Ibrahim; indeed You are Praiseworthy, Majestic. And bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You blessed Ibrahim and upon the family of Ibrahim; indeed You are Praiseworthy, Majestic' (6).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage turns to the next line of the poem, extending blessings to the Prophet's family and companions, described as guided and likened to stars by which one is guided. The commentator supports this extension by citing the hadith in which the Companions ask the Prophet how to perform the prayer upon him after the greeting of peace, since they understood the latter but not the former. The Prophet then taught them the formula pairing blessings upon Muhammad and his family with those upon Ibrahim and his family, using the divine names al-Hamid and al-Majid. The passage thus links the poem's closing formula to the precise Prophetic instruction on the wording of blessings, embedding the poetic expression within the normative Sunna.

Key Terms
آل محمد (Al Muhammad): The family of Muhammad, explicitly included alongside the Prophet in the wording of the prayer as taught in the hadith.
السلام (al-salam): The greeting of peace, distinguished by the Companions from the prayer of blessings in the hadith question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) 'Assalamu 'alayka ayyuha al-nabi'
D) 'Allahumma barik 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala ali Muhammad'

Key Takeaway
The Ibrahimic prayer formula is the sunna for sending blessings upon the Prophet and his family.

Virtue of Salat upon the Prophet
Exposition of the virtues of salat upon the Prophet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the virtues of salat upon the Prophet mentioned in the passage and explain why it is uniquely accepted by Allah.

Core Idea
Salat upon the Prophet is the only deed guaranteed acceptance because of his honor.

This opening passage of the commentary highlights the immense merits of sending blessings on the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ. The author weaves together hadith and scholarly consensus to demonstrate that unlike other acts of worship, salat is unconditionally accepted. This establishes the spiritual weight of the blessings formula that will be analyzed in subsequent lines of the poem.

Guided Path
1
List the benefits of salat
Scan the Arabic text from 'الصلاة علي نور' to 'نزول الرحمة في جم الأوقات'. Identify the specific blessings mentioned: light on the Day of Resurrection, light in the heart, light in the grave, light on the Bridge, fulfillment of needs, removal of distress, descent of mercy.
2
Note the unique acceptance
Find the phrase 'إلا الصلاة عليه جميع فإنها مقطوع بقبولها'. This shows that while other deeds may be accepted or rejected, salat upon the Prophet is definitely accepted—an exception that emphasizes its special status.
3
Extract the Friday hadith
Identify the hadith: 'إن من أفضل أيامكم يوم الجمعة فأكثروا علي من الصلاة فيه'. This reinforces the virtue of sending blessings, especially on Friday.
4
Understand the three types of salat
Note the division: salat from Allah is honor and elevation, from angels is glorification, and from humans is supplication. This clarifies the multi-faceted meaning of 'الصلاة' in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصلاة علي نور يوم القيامة ونور في القلب ونور في القبر ونور على الصراط، وقال : إن من أفضل أيامكم يوم الجمعة فأكثروا علي من الصلاة فيه. والأحاديث في فضلها جمة لا تنحصر وخصائصها لا تنضبط، فمن ذلك قضاء الحاجات وكشف الكرب المعضلات ونزول الرحمة في جم الأوقات واتفق على أن الأعمال منها مقبول ومردود إلا الصلاة عليه جميع فإنها مقطوع بقبولها إكراماً له عليه الصلاة والسلام وورد أن كل دعاء مفتتح ومختتم بها لا يرد وناهيك بهذا شرفاً وكفى به تفضيلاً والصلاة من الله تعالى زيادة تشريف وإكرام ورفع درجة وإنعام ومن الملائكة تسبيح ومنا دعاء وما من قولنا : ما دام الحجا مصدرية ظرفية أي مدة دوام الحجا يخوض من بحر المعاني لججا واللجج جمع لجة وهي البركة وفي هذا تنبيه على أنه لا يحتوي المعاني إلا الله تعالى كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِّنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاء﴾، وقال: ﴿وَفَوْقَ كُلِ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾، وقال: ﴿وَقُل رَّبِّ زِدْنِي عِلْمًا﴾ وهذا البيت من تمام البراعة المذكورة في أول بيت، وبالله تعالى على التوفيق. وآله وصحبه ذوي الهدى من شبهوا بأنجم في الاهتدا ورد في الحديث أنهم قالوا أما السلام عليك عرفناه، فكيف نصلي عليك؟ فقال: «قولوا : اللهم صلي على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد وبارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم انك حميد مجيد». فلذلك وجب أن نصلي عليه وعلى آله كما أمرنا واختلف في معنى الآل فقيل : هم أهل بيته وعترته وقيل : هم بنو هاشم وقيل : بنو عبد المطلب، واختلف في إضافته إلى الضمير فمنعها الكسائي والنحاس، وأجازها الجمهور وزعم الزبيدي أن إضافته إلى الضمير من لحن العامة، قال المرادي : والصحيح أنها من كلام العرب واختلف في الصلاة على غيره عليه الصلاة والسلام على أقوال: ثالثها الأصح تجوز بالتبعية وأما صحبه فهم كل من اجتمع وعبارة من اجتمع أولى من عبارة من رأى ليدخل مثل ابن مكتوم.
Prayer upon him is a light on the Day of Resurrection, a light in the heart, a light in the grave, and a light on the Bridge. And he said: 'Indeed, the best of your days is Friday, so send many blessings upon me on that day.' The hadiths on its virtue are numerous and unlimited, and its characteristics cannot be confined. Among them are the fulfillment of needs, the removal of distressing difficulties, and the descent of mercy in many times. It is agreed that among deeds, some are accepted and some rejected, except prayer upon him—all of it is definitely accepted, as an honor to him, peace and blessings be upon him. And it is reported that every supplication that begins and ends with it is not rejected. That suffices as honor and excellence. Prayer from Allah Almighty is an increase of honor, veneration, elevation of rank, and favor; from the angels it is glorification; and from us it is supplication. And from our saying: 'As long as al-haja (a verbal noun indicating duration, meaning the period of the duration of the mind) plunges into the sea of meanings, depths—' and al-lujaj is the plural of lujja, meaning pool. This indicates that none encompasses the meanings except Allah Almighty, as He said: 'And they encompass not anything of His knowledge except what He wills.' And He said: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing.' And He said: 'And say: My Lord, increase me in knowledge.' This line completes the eloquence mentioned in the first line. And success is from Allah Almighty alone. And his family and companions, people of guidance, likened to stars in guidance. It is reported in the hadith that they said: 'We know how to greet you, but how should we send blessings upon you?' He said: 'Say: O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad as You sent blessings upon Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim; indeed, You are Praiseworthy, Glorious. And bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You blessed Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim; indeed, You are Praiseworthy, Glorious.' Therefore, it is obligatory for us to send blessings upon him and his family as we have been commanded. There is disagreement over the meaning of 'al' (family). It is said: they are his household and his kin. It is said: they are the Banu Hashim. It is said: they are the Banu 'Abd al-Muttalib. There is disagreement over its annexation to the pronoun. Al-Kisa'i and al-Nahhas forbade it, while the majority allowed it. Al-Zubaydi claimed that its annexation to the pronoun is a solecism of the common people. Al-Muradi said: 'The correct view is that it is from the speech of the Arabs.' There is disagreement over sending blessings upon other than him, peace and blessings be upon him, in several opinions: the third, which is the most correct, permits it by way of following. As for his companions, they are everyone who met him, a believer. And the expression 'who met him' is better than the expression 'who saw him' to include someone like Ibn Maktum.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a front matter section where the author, before delving into the logical content of the Sullam poem, extols the virtues of sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, cites relevant hadiths, and then engages in a linguistic and scholarly analysis of the term 'آل' (family) used in the blessings formula. He begins by listing the benefits of salat (blessings) upon the Prophet, emphasising that unlike other deeds, it is unconditionally accepted. He then quotes a poetic line from his own poem (likely part of the Sullam) praising the Prophet's profound knowledge, with commentary explaining that only Allah encompasses all meanings. Following this, he transitions to a hadith where the Companions ask how to send blessings upon the Prophet, and the Prophet teaches them the formula including 'آل'. This leads the author to state the obligation of sending blessings upon the family as well. Then he examines the different scholarly opinions on the meaning of 'آل'—whether it refers to his household, the Banu Hashim, or the Banu 'Abd al-Muttalib. He also discusses the grammatical issue of annexing 'آل' to a pronoun: some grammarians forbid it, others allow it, and one claims it is a solecism. The author sides with the majority that it is permissible in Arabic. Finally, he touches on the ruling of sending blessings upon others and defines 'صحبه' (companions) as those who met the Prophet as believers, preferring the term 'اجتمع' over 'رأى' to include blind Companions like Ibn Maktum.

الصلاة
نور
قبول
يوم الجمعة

Meaning of 'Al' and Its Grammatical Annexation
Linguistic and legal analysis of 'آل' and 'صحبه'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different scholarly opinions on the meaning of 'آل' and the debate over its annexation to a pronoun.

Core Idea
The term 'آل' in the blessings formula is ambiguous, leading to multiple interpretations and grammatical rulings.

After establishing the virtue of salat, the author explains why it is obligatory to include 'آل' (family) based on the hadith teaching the blessings formula. He then surveys the scholarly conflict over what exactly 'آل' refers to—his household, Banu Hashim, or Banu 'Abd al-Muttalib—and the grammatical permissibility of annexing it to a pronoun. This analysis shows the precision required in worship and language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith on the blessings formula
Find the hadith: 'قولوا: اللهم صلي على محمد وعلى آل محمد...'. This is the basis for including the family in salat.
2
List the three opinions on the meaning of 'آل'
The text mentions: (1) his household and kin (أهل بيته وعترته), (2) Banu Hashim (بنو هاشم), (3) Banu 'Abd al-Muttalib (بنو عبد المطلب). Note that these differ in scope.
3
Analyze the grammatical disagreement on annexation
Some grammarians (al-Kisa'i, al-Nahhas) forbid annexing 'آل' to a pronoun; the majority allow it; al-Zubaydi calls it a solecism. Al-Muradi affirms it is correct Arabic. Understand the positions.
4
Define 'sahb' (companion) according to the author
The author defines companions as 'من اجتمع' (who met him) as believers, preferring over 'من رأى' (who saw) to include blind Companions like Ibn Maktum.

The Ladder Metaphor in al-Sullam al-Munawraq
Author presents the opening verse and explains the ladder metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ladder metaphor used in al-Sullam al-Munawraq and respond to the objection that a part of a science cannot serve as a ladder to that same science.

Core Idea
The ladder metaphor shows that easy logic is a tool for reaching difficult logic.

The author opens his poem with an invitation to take foundational principles and a declaration that he named the work 'The Adorned Ladder'. But how can a book on logic be a ladder to logic itself? This lesson unpacks the metaphor and its pedagogical purpose, showing how introductory texts serve as stepping stones to mastery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms in the opening line
Locate 'hak', 'qa'ida', 'funun', and 'sullam' in the Arabic. Note that 'hak' means 'take', 'qa'ida' is a foundational principle, 'funun' are branches, and 'sullam' is a ladder.
These terms anchor the metaphor.
2
Understand the literal meaning of sullam
The commentator explains that in the sensory world, a ladder has rungs to reach a higher surface. He quotes the Quranic verse 'Or a ladder into heaven' (Quran 6:35) to illustrate.
The Quranic usage shows the word's literal sense.
3
Grasp the metaphorical extension
In meanings, a ladder is anything that moves one from near to far. Here, the book is metaphorically a ladder because its small size and ease help students reach more advanced logic works.
The metaphor compares learning to ascending.
4
Analyse the basis of the metaphor
The author says the book is like a ladder because it facilitates understanding longer logic texts. The ease of this composition relative to others is the basis of comparison.
The ladder is not the destination but the ascent.
5
Evaluate the objection and response
The objector says: 'This book is part of logic, so it cannot be a ladder to logic.' The commentator responds: (1) It is a ladder to other logic books, not to itself; (2) within logic, easy concepts are ladders to difficult ones.
The objection fails because the ladder is relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهاك من أصوله قواعداً تجمع من فنونه فوائداً سميته بالسلم المرونق يرقى به سماء علم المنطق. هاك بمعنى خذ والقاعدة ما يبنى عليه الشيء، والفنون والفروع والضمير في سميته عائد على التأليف المفهوم من السياق، والسلم المعراج هو في الحسن ما له أدراج ليتوصل به إلى سطح وشبهه. قال تعالى : أَوْ سُلَّمًا فِي السَّمَاءِ وهو في المعاني كل ما يتوصل به من قريب إلى بعيد وهو المراد على أنه حقيقة في الحسن مجاز في المعاني، ووجه العلاقة هنا أن هذا التأليف لصغر جرمه وقربه وسهولة فهمه بالنسبة إلى غيره من مصنفات المنطق المطولة بمثابة السلم الذي يرقى به من أرض إلى سماء لأنه يعين على فهمها والدخول في علمها. فإن قلت هذا التأليف من المنطق، فكيف جعلته سلماً للمنطق؟ جزء الشيء لا يكون سلماً له. قلت المراد أن هذا الكتاب سلم لغيره من كتب المنطق كما مر، وأيضاً فإن المنطق منه سهل ومنه صعب، فالمعاني السهلة سلم للصعبة فلا اعتراض.
So take from its roots principles that gather from its branches benefits; I have named it 'The Adorned Ladder' by which one ascends to the heaven of the science of logic. 'Hak' means 'take,' and the principle is that upon which a thing is built, and the branches; and the pronoun in 'I named it' refers back to the composition understood from context. And the ladder, the stairway, in the sensory realm is what has rungs by which one reaches a surface and its like. Allah Most High said: 'Or a ladder into heaven.' And in meaning it is every means by which one moves from near to far, and this is the intended sense, though it is literal in the sensory realm and metaphorical in meanings. The basis of the relation here is that this composition, due to the smallness of its bulk, its proximity, and ease of its understanding relative to other lengthy compositions on logic, is like the ladder by which one ascends from earth to heaven, because it facilitates understanding them and entering into their science. If you say: 'This composition is from logic, so how have you made it a ladder to logic? A part of a thing is not a ladder to it.' I say: The meaning is that this book is a ladder to other books of logic, as has passed; and furthermore, logic contains easy and difficult material, so the easy meanings are a ladder to the difficult, so there is no objection.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the opening line of the author's poem and explains its key terms and central metaphor. The commentator begins with the matn itself: 'So take from its roots principles that gather from its branches benefits; I have named it the Adorned Ladder by which one ascends to the heaven of the science of logic.' He then explains that 'hak' means 'take,' that a 'qa'ida' is the foundation upon which a thing is built, that 'funun' are the branches or sections, and that the pronoun in 'samaytuhu' refers back to this composition itself. He then unpacks the word 'sullam' (ladder). In the sensory world, it is a stairway with rungs used to reach a raised surface; he cites the Quranic verse 'or a ladder into heaven.' In the realm of meaning, it is any means of moving from what is near to what is far, and this is the intended usage. The basis of the metaphor is that this composition, because of its small size, accessibility, and ease of understanding compared to other lengthy works on logic, is like a ladder that raises one from earth to heaven, since it facilitates understanding those works and entering the science. The commentator then voices an objection: how can this composition be a ladder to logic when it is itself a part of logic? A part cannot be a ladder to its own whole. He answers on two fronts. First, the book is a ladder to other books of logic, as already explained. Second, since logic contains both easy and difficult material, the easy portions serve as a ladder to the difficult, so the objection fails.

A) The book is actually about grammar, not logic.
B) The book is a ladder to other books of logic, and the easy parts of logic are a ladder to its difficult parts.
C) The book is a ladder only for students of kalam.
D) The book is not truly part of logic because it is written in verse.

Key Takeaway
The ladder metaphor teaches that introductory texts are essential stepping stones to mastering a science, and that being part of a science does not prevent it from being a ladder to further study.

Understanding the Author's Purpose: Al-Sullam as an Adorned Ladder
Author explains 'al-murawwaq' and states the book's purpose for beginners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'al-murawwaq', 'qalis', and 'al-mutawwalat' as used in the opening of al-Akhdari's Sullam, and state the author's purpose for writing the text.

Core Idea
The Sullam is an adorned ladder: a beautiful introduction that leads beginners to advanced logic.

The author al-Akhdari opens his logic poem by describing it as 'al-murawwaq' — adorned and beautified. He then prays that it be purely for Allah's sake, not deficient, and that it guide beginners to the lengthy works. In this lesson, we unpack these opening phrases and understand the pedagogical purpose behind this celebrated introduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'al-murawwaq' and its meaning
The commentator explains that 'al-murawwaq' means 'al-muzayyan' (adorned). He cites a line of poetry to support this usage, showing that the work is elegantly composed. Look at how the commentator grounds the interpretation in a poetic citation.
2
Analyze the author's supplication
The author supplicates that the work be 'khālisan' (pure) for Allah, not 'qāliṣan' (deficient). The commentator notes the grammatical point: the Name of Majesty is accusative for exaltation governed by 'arjū'. Notice the contrast between purity and deficiency.
3
Examine the purpose: ladder and door
The author describes the work as a 'sabab' (means) to the lengthy works, a 'sullam' (ladder) for ascending the degrees of this art, and a 'bāb' (door) for entering upon intricate matters. The commentator expands on these metaphors to show the work's functional role in the curriculum.
4
Understand the intended audience and outcome
The author states it is for the 'mubtadi' (beginner). The commentator affirms that whoever memorizes and understands it will have a means of entering the art, be guaranteed the solution of its important problems, and be aided in understanding its lengthy works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرونق المزين، قال الشاعر : فهذا عليه رونق الخط وحده وهذا عليه رونق الخط والملك. والله أرجو أن يكون خالصاً لوجهه الكريم ليس قالصاً وأن يكون نافعاً للمبتديء به إلى المطولات يهتدي. اسم الجلالة منصوب على التعظيم بأرجو، والقالص الناقص، ولما كان هذا سبباً إلى المطولات وسلماً يرقى به من هذا الفن درجات وباباً يدخل به من هذا الفن على المخدرات قلت في آخر البيت الثاني به إلى المطولات يهتدي، ولا شك أن من حفظه وفهمه يكون له سبباً في الدخول في هذا الفن ويضمن له حل مهماته ويعينه على فهم مطولاته.
And 'al-murawwaq' means adorned. The poet said: 'This one has the adornment of script alone, and this one has the adornment of script and kingship.' By Allah, I hope that it be pure for His noble face, not deficient, and that it be beneficial to the beginner so that by it he is guided to the lengthy works. The Name of Majesty is accusative for exaltation because of 'I hope,' and 'qalis' means deficient. Since this is a means to the lengthy works, and a ladder by which one ascends through degrees of this art, and a door by which one enters from this art upon the intricate matters, I said at the end of the second line: 'By it he is guided to the lengthy works.' There is no doubt that whoever memorizes it and understands it will have a means of entering into this art, and it guarantees for him the solution of its important problems, and aids him in understanding its lengthy works.

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'al-murawwaq' and the author's stated purpose for the work. The commentator explains that 'al-murawwaq' means 'al-muzayyan,' or adorned, and he supports this with a poetic line describing the adornment of fine handwriting. He then cites the second line of the poem, in which the author asks Allah that the work be purely for His noble face, not deficient. The commentator notes that the Divine Name is in the accusative for exaltation, governed by the verb 'arju,' and that 'qalis' means deficient or incomplete. Because this work is a means to the lengthy books, a ladder for ascending the degrees of this art, and a door for entering upon its intricate discussions, the author said at the end of the second line that by it the beginner is guided to the lengthy works. The commentator concludes by affirming that whoever memorizes and understands this work will possess a means of entering the art, will be assured of solving its important problems, and will be aided in comprehending its lengthy treatises.

Understanding the Ladder Metaphor in Logic Education
Commentator unpacks the opening poem and title of his logic primer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ladder metaphor used in the title 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq' and justify it against the objection that a part of logic cannot be a ladder to logic.

Core Idea
A logic primer is not an end in itself but a ladder to advanced works.

The commentator begins by glossing the opening words of the poem and then focuses on the title 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq' (The Adorned Ladder). He explains why a short introductory text can legitimately be called a ladder to the science of logic, anticipating and answering a potential objection. This lesson unpacks that metaphor and its pedagogical rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms in the opening line
Find the Arabic terms 'haak', 'al-qawa'id', 'al-funun' and understand their basic meanings as the commentator defines them.
'Haak' means 'take'; 'al-qāʿida' is a foundation; 'al-funūn' are the branches.
2
Analyze the ladder metaphor (al-sullam)
Note that the commentator first defines 'sullam' literally (a ladder with rungs) and then metaphorically (anything that leads from near to far). He connects this to the treatise's function.
The Quranic verse (6:35) is cited to ground the literal meaning.
3
Examine the objection and response
Identify the objection: how can a book that is part of logic be a ladder to logic? Then study the two-part response: this book is a ladder to other logic books, and easy topics in logic are a ladder to difficult ones.
This shows the author's awareness of potential circularity and his defense.
4
Recognize the purpose of the primer
From the final lines, understand that the work is meant for beginners, leading them to 'al-muṭawwalāt' (lengthy works) and resolving important problems.
The author hopes the work is pure (khāliṣ) and not deficient (qāliṣ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالله تعالى التوفيق. فهاك من أصوله قواعد تجمع من فنونه فوائد سميته بالسلم المرونق يرقى به سماء علم المنطق لأن (هاك) بمعنى خذ والقاعدة ما يبنى عليه الشيء، والفنون والفروع والضمير في (سميته) عائد على التأليف المفهوم من السياق، (والسلم) المعراج هو في الحسن ما له أدراج ليتوصل به إلى سطح وشبهه. قال تعالى : أَوْ سُلَّمًا في السَّمَاءِ وهو في المعاني كل ما يتوصل به من قريب إلى بعيد وهو المراد على أنه حقيقة في الحسن مجاز في المعاني، ووجه العلاقة هنا أن هذا التأليف لصغر جرمه وقربه وسهولة فهمه بالنسبة إلى غيره من مصنفات المنطق المطولة بمثابة السلم الذي يرقى به من أرض إلى سماء لأنه يعين على فهمها والدخول في علمها فإن قلت هذا التأليف من المنطق، فكيف جعلته سلماً للمنطق؟ جزء الشيء لا يكون سلماً له قلت المراد أن هذا الكتاب سلم لغيره من كتب المنطق كما مر وأيضاً فإن المنطق منه سهل ومنه صعب، فالمعاني السهلة سلم للصعبة فلا اعتراض والمرونق المزين، قال الشاعر : فهذا عليه رونق الخط وحده وهذا عليه رونق الخط والملك والله أرجو أن يكون خالصاً لوجهه الكريم ليس قالصاً وأن يكون نافعاً للمبتديء به إلى المطولات يهتدي اسم الجلالة منصوب على التعظيم بأرجو، والقالص الناقص، ولما كان هذا سبباً إلى المطولات وسلماً يرقى به من هذا الفن درجات وباباً يدخل به من هذا الفن على المخدرات قلت في آخر البيت الثاني «به إلى المطولات يهتدي»، ولا شك أن من حفظه وفهمه يكون له سبباً في الدخول في هذا الفن ويضمن له حل مهماته ويعينه على فهم مطولاته.
And with Allah the Exalted is success. So here, from its principles are rules that gather from its branches benefits; I named it 'The Adorned Ladder' by which the heaven of the science of logic is ascended, because 'haak' means 'take,' and the rule is that upon which a thing is built, and al-funun are the branches, and the pronoun in 'samaytuhu' returns to the composition understood from the context. And al-sullam is the ladder; in the sensory realm it is that which has rungs by which one reaches a roof and its like. Allah the Exalted said: 'or a ladder into heaven.' And in meanings it is everything by which one proceeds from what is near to what is far, and it is the intended meaning, such that it is literal in the sensory and metaphorical in meanings. And the aspect of the relation here is that this composition, due to the smallness of its bulk and its closeness and ease of its understanding relative to other lengthy works on logic, is like the ladder by which one ascends from earth to heaven because it aids in understanding them and entering into their science. So if you say: this composition is from logic, so how did you make it a ladder to logic? A part of a thing is not a ladder to it. I say: the intended meaning is that this book is a ladder to other books of logic, as passed. And furthermore, logic has an easy aspect and a difficult aspect, so the easy meanings are a ladder to the difficult ones; so there is no objection. And al-murawnaq means adorned. The poet said: 'So this one has the adornment of calligraphy alone, and this one has the adornment of calligraphy and sovereignty.' And [by] Allah, I hope that it be purely for His Noble Face, not deficient, and that it be beneficial for the beginner; by it he is guided to the lengthy works. The Name of Majesty is in the accusative for magnification because of 'arju.' And al-qaas means deficient. And since this is a means to the lengthy works and a ladder by which one ascends in this art degrees, and a door by which one enters from this art into the abstruse matters, I said in the last line of the second couplet: 'by it to the lengthy works he is guided.' And there is no doubt that whoever memorizes it and understands it will have, by it, a means for entering into this art, and it guarantees for him the resolution of its important problems, and it aids him in understanding its lengthy works.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe in which the commentator unpacks the opening poem of his primer, explaining the title al-Sullam al-Munawraq and the author's purpose. He begins by glossing each word of the first line: haak means take; qawa'id are the foundations upon which a thing is built; al-funun refers to the branches; and the pronoun in samaytuhu refers back to the composition itself. He then turns to the word al-sullam, noting that in the sensory realm it is a ladder with rungs leading to a roof, citing the Quranic verse or a ladder into heaven. In the realm of meaning, he explains, it is anything that serves as a means from what is near to what is far, and this is the intended sense, so the book is a ladder metaphorically. He clarifies the basis of the comparison: because this treatise is small, accessible, and easy to understand relative to the lengthy works of logic, it functions like a ladder from earth to heaven, helping the student understand those works and enter the science. He then addresses a natural objection: how can a book that is itself part of logic be a ladder to logic? Would not a part of something be incapable of serving as a ladder to the whole? He responds first by stating that the intended meaning is that this book is a ladder to other books of logic, as already mentioned. Second, he adds that within logic itself there are easy and difficult topics, and the easy ones serve as a ladder to the difficult ones, so no objection remains. Moving to the word al-murawnaq, he defines it as adorned and cites a poetic line contrasting the adornment of calligraphy alone with calligraphy accompanied by royal elegance. He then expresses his hope that the work be purely for the sake of Allah's Noble Face, explaining the grammar: the Name of Allah is in the accusative case as an object of an implied oath because of the verb arju. He defines al-qaas as deficient, negating it for the book. Finally, he explains the last line of the second couplet, by it to the lengthy works he is guided, noting that since this work is a means to the lengthy books, a ladder ascending the degrees of this art, and a door entering into its subtle abstruse matters, whoever memorizes and understands it will have a means to enter the art, guarantee resolution of its difficult problems, and find aid in comprehending its lengthy works.

Checkpoint

A) He argues that a part of a science can never be a ladder to the whole, so the title is merely a decorative metaphor without real basis.
B) He holds that the treatise is a ladder to other lengthy books of logic, and that easy topics within logic serve as a ladder to difficult ones.
C) He claims that the treatise replaces all longer works of logic and renders them unnecessary for the student.

Key Takeaway

Three Opinions on Studying Logic
Author classifies three opinions on studying logic and states his preferred view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on studying logic and explain the condition the author sets for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Logic is permissible only for the grounded student, lest it lead to heresy.

This passage addresses a foundational question for any student of Islamic sciences: is it permissible to study logic? The author presents three opinions—prohibition, recommendation, and conditional permissibility—and then states his own view, which emphasizes the importance of a strong scriptural foundation. Understanding this debate helps you navigate the role of rational tools in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and list the three positions: Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited it; a group (led by al-Ghazali) recommended it; and the author's preferred view that it is permissible under a condition.
2
Extract the condition for permissibility
Find the description of the student for whom logic is permissible: one with a sharp natural disposition, sound mind, sound temperament, and practiced in the Book and Sunna.
The condition is designed to prevent the student from being misled by fanciful methods.
3
Understand the warning
The author warns that without grounding, students may corrupt premises and syllogisms, leading to heresy like the Mu'tazila and Qadariyya. Reflect on how this connects to the condition.
4
Appreciate the purpose
The author explains that this chapter is meant to give the beginner insight into the controversy. Understand that the lesson is not just about prohibition but about responsible inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في جواز الاشتغال به. حرما. والخلف في جواز الاشتغال به على ثلاثة أقوال فابن الصلاح والنواوي وقال قوم ينبغي أن يعلما والقولة المشهورة الصحيحة جوازه لكامل القريحة ممارس السنة والكتاب ليهتدي به إلى الصواب. هذا الفصل موضوع لذكر الخلاف المذكور في جواز الاشتغال بعلم المنطق ليكون المبتديء على بصيرة من مقصوده. وقد اختلف فيه على ثلاثة أقوال كما ذكر: فمنعه النووي وابن الصلاح، واستحبه الغزالي(۱) ومن تبعه قائلاً: «من لا يعرفه لا يوثق بعلمه» والمختار الصحيح جوازه لذكي القريحة صحيح الذهن سليم الطبع ممارس الكتاب والسنة لئلا يؤول إلى اتباع بعض الطرق الوهمية فيفسد المقدمات والأقيسة النظرية فتزل قدمه في بعض الدركات السفلية، ومنه ضلت المعتزلة (۲) والقدرية (۳) وغيرهم من الطوائف البدعية فخاضوا في ذلك حتى بدلوا وغيروا في السنة الشرعية والملة المحمدية فباءوا بضلالة جلية وجهالة غبية. اللهم وفقنا لاتباع النبيين وتوفنا مسلمين لا مبدلين ولا مغيرين يا رب العالمين.
A chapter on the permissibility of occupying oneself with it. They two forbade it. And the disagreement on the permissibility of occupying oneself with it is upon three opinions: so Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi [held that], and a group said that it ought to be learned, and the well-known correct opinion is its permissibility for one of complete natural disposition, practiced in the Sunna and the Book, so that he may be guided by it to correctness. This chapter is placed to mention the disagreement mentioned regarding the permissibility of occupying oneself with the science of logic so that the beginner may have insight into his purpose. And there has been disagreement about it upon three opinions as mentioned: so al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah forbade it, and al-Ghazali (1) and whoever followed him recommended it saying: 'One who does not know it, his knowledge is not trusted.' And the chosen correct [view] is its permissibility for one of sharp natural disposition, sound mind, sound temperament, practiced in the Book and the Sunna, lest he fall into following some fanciful methods, so he corrupts the premises and theoretical syllogisms, so his foot slips into some of the lowest levels. And from this the Muʿtazila (2) and the Qadariyya (3) and others from the heretical sects went astray, so they plunged into that until they altered and changed the prophetic Sunna and the Muhammadan religion, so they returned with manifest misguidance and foolish ignorance. O Allah, grant us success in following the prophets and cause us to die as Muslims, not as alterers or changers, O Lord of the Worlds.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is methodological and introductory; it establishes the author’s stance on the study of logic before entering the technical material. The author opens with a chapter heading indicating that the subject is whether it is permissible to occupy oneself with logic. He notes in summary form that Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi forbade it, that another group held it ought to be learned, and that the well-known correct opinion is its permissibility for one of complete natural disposition who is already practiced in the Sunna and the Book, so that logic becomes an instrument guiding him to correctness. He then explains the purpose of this chapter: to alert the beginner to the controversy so that he understands what is at stake. He repeats the three opinions in expanded form: prohibition by al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah, recommendation by al-Ghazali and his followers—who argued that a scholar not versed in logic is not trustworthy in his knowledge—and the author’s own preferred view, which is conditional permissibility. The condition is stringent: the student must be of sharp intelligence, sound mind, sound temperament, and grounded in the Book and Sunna. The reason for this caution is that without such grounding, the student may follow fanciful methods, corrupt his premises and theoretical syllogisms, and slip into grave error. The author then gives a historical warning: the Muʿtazila and Qadariyya and other heretical sects fell into precisely this danger; they immersed themselves in rational methods without the necessary scriptural anchor, and ended up altering and changing the prophetic Sunna and the Muhammadan religion, returning with manifest misguidance. The passage closes with a supplication for steadfastness.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition for the permissibility of studying logic?
A) It is permissible for anyone who finds it interesting.
B) It is permissible only for one with sharp intelligence, sound mind, and grounding in the Book and Sunna.
C) It is forbidden for everyone except hadith scholars.
D) It is recommended unconditionally for all students of kalam.

Key Takeaway
The study of logic is permissible only for those who are already grounded in the Quran and Sunna, because without such grounding it can lead to heresy.
