Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Three Views on Studying Logic: Permissibility and Precautions
Author presents three views on studying logic and adopts the middle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on studying logic and explain the author's chosen view and its conditions.

Core Idea
Logic is not inherently forbidden: its permissibility depends on the student's grounding in revelation.

The classical scholars disagreed on whether studying formal logic is permissible. Some forbade it outright; others made it a condition for reliable knowledge; and a third group permitted it with strict conditions. Understanding this disagreement is essential for situating logic within the Islamic sciences and for appreciating the care with which Muslim scholars approached its study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Locate the three opinions in the Arabic passage. The first forbids logic (al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah). The second recommends it (al-Ghazali and his followers). The third is the author's chosen view, which permits it conditionally.
The terms 'man'ahu' (forbade), 'istahabba' (recommended), and 'al-mukhtar al-sahih' (correct chosen) signal the positions.
2
Analyse the author's conditions
The author lists four qualities for permissibility: sharp intellect, sound mind, sound natural disposition, and being practised in the Book and Sunnah. Understand why each is necessary to avoid fallacious methods.
These conditions are cumulative; missing one increases the risk of error.
3
Understand the warning
The author warns that without these safeguards, a student may follow fallacious methods, corrupt the premises and theoretical syllogisms, and slip into grievous error. Note the consequence: 'tazillu qadamuhu' (his foot slips).
This echoes a famous Quranic phrase about slipping from the path.
4
Connect to historical examples
The author cites the Mu'tazila and Qadariyya as those who studied logic without proper grounding and altered the Sunnah. Reflect on how this serves as a concrete warning.
The terms 'baddalu' and 'ghayyaru' (altered and changed) are strong indictments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الفصل موضوع لذكر الخلاف المذكور في جواز الاشتغال بعلم المنطق ليكون المبتديء على بصيرة من مقصوده. وقد اختلف فيه على ثلاثة أقوال كما ذكر: فمنعه النووي وابن الصلاح، واستحبه الغزالي ومن تبعه قائلاً: من لا يعرفه لا يوثق بعلمه. والمختار الصحيح جوازه لذي القريحة صحيح الذهن سليم الطبع ممارس الكتاب والسنة لئلا يؤول إلى اتباع بعض الطرق الوهمية فيفسد المقدمات والأقيسة النظرية فتزل قدمه في بعض الدركات السفلية، ومنه ضلت المعتزلة والقدرية وغيرهم من الطوائف البدعية فخاضوا في ذلك حتى بدلوا وغيروا في السنة الشرعية والملة المحمدية فباءوا بضلالة جلية وجهالة غبية. اللهم وفقنا لاتباع النبيين وتوفنا مسلمين لا مبدلين ولا مغيرين يا رب العالمين.
This chapter is designated for mentioning the disagreement mentioned concerning the permissibility of occupying oneself with the science of logic so that the beginner may have insight into his purpose. And [people] have differed concerning it into three opinions as he mentioned: so al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah forbade it, and al-Ghazali and whoever followed him recommended it, saying: whoever does not know it, his knowledge is not trusted. And the correct chosen [view] is its permissibility for one of sharp natural intellect, sound mind, sound natural disposition, practised in the Book and the Sunnah, lest he resort to following some fallacious methods and corrupt the premises and theoretical syllogisms, so his foot slips into some of the low levels; and from it [i.e., logic studied wrongly] the Mu'tazila and the Qadariyya and others from the heretical sects went astray, so they plunged into that until they altered and changed the Prophetic Sunnah and the Muhammadan religion, so they returned with manifest misguidance and foolish ignorance. Our Lord, grant us success in following the Prophets and cause us to die as Muslims, not as those who alter or change, O Lord of the Worlds.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a prefatory methodological statement in which the author explains why he opens with a discussion of the disagreement surrounding the study of logic. He states that this section is intended to give the beginner insight into the purpose of the science. He reports three positions: prohibition, represented by al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah; recommendation, represented by al-Ghazali and his followers, who said that whoever does not know logic is not trusted in his knowledge; and the author's own chosen view, which is permissibility, but only for a person who possesses sharp natural intelligence, a sound mind, a sound disposition, and who is thoroughly practised in the Book and the Sunnah. The author warns that without these safeguards, a student may follow fallacious methods, corrupt the premises of theoretical syllogisms, and slip into grievous error. He then links this danger to the historical deviation of the Mu'tazila and the Qadariyya and other heretical sects, stating that they plunged into logic without the necessary grounding, altered the Prophetic Sunnah and the Muhammadan religion, and thus met with manifest misguidance and foolish ignorance. The passage concludes with a supplication for steadfastness upon the way of the Prophets without alteration or change.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of studying logic is conditional on the student's intellectual and scriptural grounding; otherwise it leads to corruption of reasoning and deviation from the Sunnah.
